Sawm — Fasting
beginner
8 min
Types of Sawm and Intention for Fixed-Time Fasts
Definition of sawm, classification, and intention ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify fasting into its types (wajib/nafl, time-specific) and state the Hanafi rule on intention timing for Ramadan and specified nadhr fasts.

Core Idea
Intention for fixed-time fasts is valid before zawal even if missed at night.

This lesson defines the legal concept of sawm (fasting) and its categories in the Hanafi school. You will learn the difference between obligatory and supererogatory fasts, and the special ruling that allows making intention for Ramadan and specified nadhr fasts until midday if the night intention was missed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is sawm and what is the hukm of Ramadan fasts? The passage defines sawm as refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual relations, and establishes Ramadan fasts as fard.
Evidence is Quran 2:183 and hadith.
2
Classify the types of fasting
Fasting is divided into wajib and nafl. Wajib further divides into time-specific (Ramadan, specified nadhr) and non-time-specific (qada, unspecified nadhr, kaffarah). Nafl fasts are supererogatory.
Six total types are enumerated in the commentary.
3
Understand the evidence for obligation
The Quranic text 'kutiba' indicates obligation per Hanafi usul. The hadith confirms Ramadan as the prescribed month.
Dalil is dalil_naqli.
4
Apply the intention rule for fixed-time fasts
For Ramadan and specified nadhr, intention can be made from the night. If missed, intention before zawal (midday) suffices, provided one has abstained from nullifiers from dawn.
The justification is the legal maxim 'al-hukm li al-akthar' (the rule of the majority).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad required intention before half the day; the mufta bihi is before zawal. Shafi'is and Malikis require intention before fajr for Ramadan.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; the Hanafi position is based on ease.

[۵۵۱] (الصوم ضربان واجب و نفل فالواجب ضربان ما يتعلق بزمان بعينه كصوم رمضان والنذر المعين [۵۵۲] (۲) فيجوز صومه بنية من اليل فان لم ينو حتى اصبح کتاب الصوم ده
ضروری نوٹ صوم کے معنی رکنا ہے۔ روزہ میں کھانے، پینے اور جماع سے رکنا ہے اس لئے اس کو صوم کہتے ہیں۔ روزہ فرض ہونے کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے یا ایها الذين آمنوا كتب عليكم الصيام كما كتب على الذين من قبلكم لعلكم تتقون (الف) ( آیت ۱۸۳ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اور حدیث میں ہے ان اعرابیا جاء الی رسول الله الله ... فقال اخبرني ماذا فرض الله على من الصيام فقال شهر رمضان الا ان تطوع شيئا (ب) ( بخاری شریف، کتاب الصوم، باب وجوب صوم رمضان ص ۲۵۴ نمبر (۱۸۹) اس آیت اور حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ رمضان کے روزے فرض ہیں۔ [۵۵۱] (۱) روزے کی دو قسمیں ہیں واجب اور نفل ، پس واجب کی دو قسمیں ہیں، ان میں سے ایک جو تعلق رکھتی ہے متعین زمانے کے ساتھ جیسے رمضان کے روزے اور نذرمعین۔ روزے کی چھ قسمیں ہیں (۱) رمضان کے روزے (۲) نذرمعین کا روزہ (۳) قضاء رمضان (۴) نذر غیر معین (۵) کفارات کے روزے (۶) نفل روزے۔ ان چھ قسموں میں سے پہلی دو قسمیں رمضان کے روزے اور نذر معین وقت متعین کے ساتھ ہیں اور باقی چار میں وقت کے ساتھ متعین نہیں ہے۔ کسی دن بھی رکھ سکتے ہیں۔ [۵۵۲] (۲) وقت متعین کا روزہ رات کی نیت کے ساتھ جائز ہے، پس اگر نیت نہ کی ہو یہاں تک کہ صبح ہو گئی تو اس کو کافی ہوگی وی نیست جو رات اور زوال کے درمیان کی گئی ہے۔
ر اگر رات کو نیت نہ کی ہو تو زوال سے پہلے نیت کر لی تو وہ نیت بھی رمضان کے روزے کے لئے اور نذرمعین کے ادا ہونے کے لئے کافی ہے۔ کیونکہ رمضان کا مہینہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ طے ہے کہ ایک مسلمان کو روزہ رکھنا ہے اور صبح سے زوال تک کھایا پیا بھی نہیں ہے اس لئے اکثر دن میں نیت کر لی تو روزہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔ اور زوال سے پہلے نیت کر لی تو آدھا دن سے زیادہ نیت پائی گئی لا کر حکم الکل کے قاعدہ کے اعتبار سے کافی ہو جائے گی ۔ یہی حال نذر معین کا ہے کہ پہلے سے روزہ رکھنے کے لئے دن متعین ہے اس لئے یہی گمان ہے کہ اپنے وعدے کے
مطابق روزہ رکھے گا۔ روزہ کا وقت صبح صادق سے شروع ہوتا ہے اس لئے صبح صادق سے آدھا دن سے زیادہ کا اعتبار کرنا ہوگا۔
حاشیہ : (الف) اے ایمان والو تم پر روزہ فرض کیا گیا ہے جیسا کہ تم سے پہلے لوگوں پر فرض کیا گیا ہے، شاید کہ تم تقوی اختیار کرو (ب) دیہاتی نے کہا مجھ کو خبر دیجئے اللہ نے مجھ پر روزے میں کیا فرض کیا ہے۔ آپ نے فرمایا رمضان کے روزے ۔ مگر یہ کہ تم نفلی روزے رکھنا چا ہو۔
Book of Fasting. [551] (Fasting is of two types: obligatory and supererogatory. The obligatory is of two types: one that is tied to a specific time, like the fast of Ramadan and a specified nadhr (vowed fast). [552] (2) It is permissible to observe such a fast with intention from the night. If one did not intend until morning... Book of Fasting. Essential Note: The meaning of sawm is to refrain. In fasting, refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse is called sawm. The evidence for fasting being fard is this verse: 'O you who have believed, decreed upon you is fasting as it was decreed upon those before you that you may become righteous' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:183). And in the hadith: A bedouin came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and said: 'Tell me what Allah has prescribed for me in terms of fasting.' He said: 'The month of Ramadan, unless you voluntarily fast more.' From this verse and hadith, it is known that the fasts of Ramadan are fard. [551] (1) There are two types of fasting: wajib and nafl. Then the wajib are of two types: one that is tied to a specific time, like the fasts of Ramadan and a specified nadhr. There are six types of fasting: (1) Ramadan fasts, (2) specified nadhr fast, (3) make-up of Ramadan, (4) unspecified nadhr fast, (5) expiation fasts, (6) nafl fasts. Among these six, the first two types—Ramadan and specified nadhr—are tied to a specific time; the remaining four are not tied to a specific time—they can be observed on any day. [552] (2) A fixed-time fast is valid with intention from the night. So if one did not intend until morning, it will suffice that which is made between night and zawal (midday). If one did not intend at night, then they intend before zawal, that intention is sufficient for the fast of Ramadan and for performing a specified nadhr. Because due to it being the month of Ramadan, it is determined that a Muslim must fast, and from dawn to zawal they have not eaten or drunk anything, so if they intend during most of the day, the fast will be valid. And if they intend before zawal, the intention is found for more than half the day, so by the rule of 'the majority takes the ruling of the whole', it will suffice. The same applies to a specified nadhr: the day is predetermined for fasting, so the expectation is that they will fast according to their vow. The time of fasting begins from true dawn, so it must be considered that more than half the day from true dawn is found.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Fasting with a definition and classification. It first defines 'sawm' (fasting) as refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual relations. The obligatory nature of Ramadan fasts is established by Quran (2:183) and a hadith where a bedouin asks the Prophet, who replies 'the month of Ramadan, unless you voluntarily add more'. The passage then classifies fasting: sawm is divided into wajib (obligatory) and nafl (supererogatory). Wajib fasting is further divided into those tied to a specific time (like Ramadan fasts and a specified vowed fast) and those not tied to a specific time (like make-up fasts, unspecified nadhr, kaffarah, and nafl). The passage then gives the ruling on intention for fixed-time fasts (Ramadan and specified nadhr): it is permissible to intend from the night. If one fails to intend at night, an intention made before zawal (midday) is sufficient. The reasoning is that since Ramadan is designated, the Muslim is expected to fast, and from dawn to zawal one has abstained from nullifiers. Making intention before zawal means that most of the day is covered by intention, and by the legal maxim 'the rule applies to the whole based on the majority' (al-hukm li al-akthar), the intention is valid. The same applies to a specified nadhr because the day is fixed for fulfilling the vow.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Linguistically: to refrain. Legally: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention.
نذر معين (nadhr mu'ayyan): A vow in which the person specifies a particular day or days for fasting (e.g., 'I will fast on Monday if...').
زوال (zawal): Midday; when the sun passes its zenith; the point after which the time for zuhr begins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the latest time one can make intention for a Ramadan fast if the night intention was missed?
A) At fajr (dawn)
B) Before zawal (midday)
C) Before asr (afternoon)
D) Before sunset

Key Takeaway
Fasting is classified into time-specific and non-time-specific; intention for time-specific fasts can be made before zawal if missed at night.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Types of Fasting and Intention for Specified Obligatory Fasts
Defines fasting categories and ruling of intention for time-specific obligatory fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main categories of fasting according to the Hanafi school and apply the ruling that for time-specific obligatory fasts, intention may be made before zawal if not intended at night.

Core Idea
For specified obligatory fasts like Ramadan, intention before zawal suffices if no food was consumed.

Fasting is divided into obligatory and supererogatory, with obligatory further split into time-specific and non-time-specific. The Hanafi school permits a late intention for time-specific fasts, a dispensation not accepted by all schools. This lesson examines the classification and the ruling based on the hadith of Hafsa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of fasting
From the passage, state the two main divisions (wajib and nafl) and the two types of wajib: those tied to a specific time (Ramadan, nadhr mu'ayyan) and those not.
Non-time-specific wajib fasts include qada, nadhr mutlaq, and kaffarat.
2
State the general rule for intention
For time-specific obligatory fasts, intention must be made during the night. But if forgotten, intention before zawal is valid provided no invalidating action (eating, drinking, etc.) occurred.
Zawal is when the sun passes its zenith.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The hadith of Hafsa (RA) 'Whoever does not intend the fast before dawn, there is no fast for him' is interpreted restrictively. The Hanafis extend 'before dawn' to before zawal based on the practice of 'Ashura and istihsan.
The Shafi'i school requires night intention for all obligatory fasts.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that any obligatory fast requires night intention, citing the same hadith strictly. The Hanafi response is that the hadith is ahad and can be overridden by stronger evidence and common practice.
The practical consequence: a Hanafi can save a fast with late intention; a Shafi'i would need to make it up.

Al-Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 1, page 349. Book of Fasting. [551] Fasting is of two types: obligatory and supererogatory. Obligatory is of two types: that which is connected to a specific time, like the fast of Ramadan and a specified vow. [552] It is permissible to fast it with an intention from the night, and if he did not intend until morning... (Urdu explanation follows: The meaning of sawm is to refrain. The evidence for fasting being obligatory is the verse and hadith. There are six types of fasts, etc.)
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-Sawm by defining the legal categories of fasting. The author first states the general principle: fasting (sawm) is divided into obligatory (wajib) and supererogatory (nafl). Obligatory fasting is further subdivided into two types: (1) those tied to a specific time, such as Ramadan or a vowed fast where a specific day is designated (nadhr mu'ayyan), and (2) those that are an absolute obligation on the person's liability (dhimmah) like qada', nadhr mutlaq, and kaffarat (discussed later). The ruling for time-specific obligatory fasts is that the intention (niyyah) must be made during the night—before the break of dawn—but if one forgets or fails to do so, it is still valid to intend from dawn until just before zawal (midday) provided one has not eaten, drunk, or engaged in sexual intercourse during that period. The author supports this with the hadith of Hafsa (RA) and the practical example of 'Ashura. The commentary in Urdu elaborates that because Ramadan is a fixed month, it is presumed that a Muslim intends to fast, so a late intention before zawal is acceptable. The same applies to nadhr mu'ayyan. This is a clear statement of the Hanafi position that for specified obligatory fasts, the time for intention extends from nightfall to just before zawal.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Linguistically: to refrain. Legally: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention.
نذر معين (nadhr mu'ayyan): A vow to fast on a specific day, e.g., 'I will fast on Friday.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the latest permissible time to make the intention for a Ramadan fast if one did not intend during the night?
A) Before Fajr only
B) Before zawal (midday), provided no food/drink was consumed after dawn
C) Any time before Maghrib
D) Only during the night

Key Takeaway
For time-specific obligatory fasts, the Hanafi school allows intention to be made after dawn as long as it is before zawal and no invalidating action has occurred.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
Intention for Non-Specified Obligatory and Supererogatory Fasts
Rules of intention for absolute obligatory and nafl fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that non-specified obligatory fasts (qada', kaffarat, nadhr mutlaq) require intention from the night while nafl fasts permit intention before zawal.

Core Idea
A fast established as a liability requires night intention; a nafl fast may be intended up to midday.

This passage completes the classification of fasts by addressing the second type: those that are a pure liability on the person. Unlike fasting in Ramadan or a specified vow, these fasts have no fixed day; they are merely a debt to be fulfilled. The Hanafi school determines that such fasts require the intention to be made during the night, before dawn. In contrast, supererogatory fasts allow flexibility: you may set your intention even after dawn, as long as it's before midday.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of fasts
The passage distinguishes between fasts that are time-specific (e.g., Ramadan, specified vow) and those that are liability-based (qada', kaffarat, absolute vow). Classify the given fast correctly.
Liability fasts have no fixed time; they are debts.
2
State the ruling for liability-based fasts
For liability fasts, the intention must be made during the night, from sunset to dawn. If intention is made after dawn, the fast is invalid.
The hadith of Hafsa is cited as proof.
3
State the ruling for nafl fasts
For supererogatory fasts, intention can be made any time before zawal (midday), even after dawn, provided no invalidating action has occurred.
The 'Ashura hadith shows the Prophet allowed daytime intention for nafl.
4
Examine the proofs
The hadith of Hafsa (requires pre-dawn intention) is applied to liability fasts. The 'Ashura hadith (command to fast after dawn) is applied to nafl and, by qiyas, to time-specific obligatory fasts.
The Hanafi school uses qiyas to extend the 'Ashura ruling to specified obligatory fasts.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school requires night intention for all obligatory fasts, including nafl when vowed. The Hanafi school distinguishes between specified and unspecified obligations.
This ikhtilaf is based on different interpretations of the same hadith.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الاول
۳۵۰
كتاب الصوم
اجزأته النية ما بينه وبين الزوال [۵۵۳] (۳) والضرب الثانى ما يثبت في الذمة كقضاء رمضان والنذر المطلق والكفارات فلا يجوز صومه الا بنية من الليل وكذلك صوم الظهار . [۵۵۴] (۴) والنفل كله يجوز بنية قبل الزوال. النذر المعین : کوئی آدمی نذر مانے کہ مثلا جمعہ کے دن روزہ رکھوں گا تو چونکہ جمعہ کا دن روزہ رکھنے کے لئے متعین کیا اس لئے یہ نذر معین ہوئی ، نذرواجب ہونے کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے ثم ليقضوا تفثهم وليوفوا نذورهم (الف ) ( آیت ۲۹ سورۃ الحج ۲۲) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ نذر مانی ہو تو اس کو پوری کرنا چاہئے ۔ روزہ متعین ہے، دن کو بھی نیت کر لے تو جائز ہو جائے گا۔ اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے عن حفصة زوج النبى الله الله ان رسول الله قال من لم يجمع الصيام قبل الفجر فلا صيام له (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب في النية في الصوم ص ۳۴۰ نمبر ۲۴۵۴ / ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء لا صيام لمن لم يعزم من المليل ص ۱۳۵ نمبر ۷۳۰ ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ رات سے روزے کی نیت کرنی چاہئے (۲) دوسری حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ متعین روزہ اور نفل روزہ کی نیت زوال سے پہلے بھی کرے گا تو روزہ درست ہو جائے گا عن سلمة بن اكوع ان النبى الله بعث رجلا ينادى فى الناس يوم عاشوراء ان من اكل فليتم او فليصم ومن لم يأكل فلا ياكل (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب اذ انوی بالنھار صوما (۲۵۷ نمبر ۱۹۲۴ را بود اؤ د شریف، باب فی الرخصة فیه ای فی البیت ) ص ۳۴۰ نمبر ۲۴۵۵) اس حدیث میں جس نے دوپہر تک کھایا نہیں تھا اس کو نیت کر کے روزہ رکھنے کا حکم دیا جس سے معلوم ہوا
کہ دو پہر سے پہلے روزہ کی نیت کر سکتا ہے۔ حدیث میں یہ حکم سنت روزے کا ہے لیکن متعین روزے کو بھی اسی پر قیاس کیا جائے گا۔ (۵۵۳](۳) دوسری قسم وہ روزہ ہے جو ذمہ میں ثابت ہو جیسے رمضان کی قضا اور نذر مطلق اور کفارات کے روزے، پس جائز نہیں ہے اس کے روزے مگر رات کی نیت کے ساتھ ، اور ایسے ہی کفارہ ظہار کے روزے۔
ہونگے۔
وہ روزے جو وقت کے ساتھ متعین نہیں ہیں اور نفل بھی نہیں ہیں، ان روزوں کی نیت رات سے ہی کرنی ہوگی ، تب روزے درست نی ہوگی جب
چونکہ یہ روزے مطلق وقت کے ساتھ ہیں، کسی وقت کے ساتھ متعین نہیں ہے اس لئے رات ہی سے نیت کر کے واجب روزہ متعین کرنا ہوگا ۔ اور رات ہی سے نیت کرنی ہوگی۔
او پر ابوداؤد کی حدیث ہے۔ ان رسول الله الله قال من لم يجمع الصيام قبل الفجر فلا صيام له (ابوداؤد شریف، نمبر ۲۴۵۴ / ترمذی شریف نمبر ۷۳۰ ) [۵۵۴) (۴) اور نقل کل کے کل جائز ہے زوال کے پہلے کی نیت سے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) پھر اپنی پراگندگی کو دور کرو اور اپنی نذر پوری کرو (ب) آپ نے فرمایا جو آدمی فجر سے پہلے روزے کا پختہ ارادہ نہ کرے اس کا روزہ نہیں ہوا (ج) آپ نے ایک آدمی کو عاشورہ کے دن لوگوں میں اعلان کرنے کے لئے بھیجا کہ جس نے کھانا کھایا وہ روزہ پورا کرے یا روزہ رکھے ۔ فرمایا اور جس نے کھانا نہیں کھایا تو اب نہ کھائے یعنی روزہ رکھے۔
Al-Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 1, page 350. Book of Fasting. [553] The second type is that which is established in the liability (dhimmah), like qada' of Ramadan, absolute vow (nadhr mutlaq), and expiations (kaffarat). It is not permissible to fast it except with intention from the night, and likewise the fast of zihar. [554] As for supererogatory (nafl) fasting, all of it is permissible with intention before zawal. (Urdu explanation: The obligation of a vow is proven by the verse... The hadith of Hafsa shows night intention is required; the hadith of 'Ashura shows that for specified and nafl fasts, intention before zawal suffices.)
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage completes the author's classification of fasting by addressing the second type of obligatory fast: those that are an absolute liability on the person (ma yathbutu fi al-dhimmah). These include: qada' of missed Ramadan days, an unconditional vow (nadhr mutlaq) where no specific day is named, and expiatory fasts (kaffarat) such as for breaking an oath, for zihar (an ancient form of divorce pronouncement), and for intentional violation of Ramadan. Unlike the first type (time-specific), these fasts have no fixed time; they are merely a debt on the person. Therefore, the intention must be made during the night—from sunset to dawn—and the fast cannot be initiated with a daytime intention. If one intends after dawn, the fast is not valid. The author then states the rule for supererogatory (nafl) fasts: intention can be made at any time before zawal (midday), even after dawn, provided no invalidating action has occurred. The commentary in Urdu reinforces these rulings with the hadith of Hafsa (requiring pre-dawn intention) and the hadith of 'Ashura (which shows that the Prophet allowed daytime intention for nafl and, by qiyas, for specified obligatory fasts). Thus the Hanafi ruling distinguishes sharply between time-specific obligatory fasts (which allow daytime intention before zawal), absolute liability obligatory fasts (which require night intention), and nafl fasts (which only require intention before zawal).

Key Terms
نذر مطلق (nadhr mutlaq): An absolute or unconditional vow to fast, without specifying a particular day.
ظهار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a husband says to his wife 'You are to me like my mother's back.' It requires expiation, including fasting for two consecutive months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of fast allows intention to be made during the daytime (before zawal) even after dawn?
A) Qada' of Ramadan
B) Kaffarat for zihar
C) Nafl (supererogatory) fast
D) Nadhr mutlaq

Key Takeaway
For Hanafis, liability fasts require night intention; nafl fasts permit intention before zawal.

Seeking the Crescent on 29th Sha'ban
Directs to seek the new moon on the 29th of Sha'ban

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when to begin Ramadan fasting and explain the requirement of actual moon sighting or completing 30 days of Sha'ban.

Core Idea
Fasting Ramadan begins with confirmed sighting; if unseen, complete Sha'ban as 30 days.

Determining the start of Ramadan is a practical obligation for every Muslim. This lesson presents the classic Hanafi ruling: the crescent must be sighted on the 29th of Sha'ban. If not seen, the month is completed to 30 days. The proof is a direct command from the Prophet ﷺ, emphasizing visual confirmation over calculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage commands seeking the crescent on the 29th of Sha'ban. Identify that this act is a sunnah muakkadah, and the resulting fasting is wajib upon sighting or completion of 30 days.
Seeking is recommended, not obligatory; but fasting becomes obligatory once the month is confirmed.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Ibn 'Umar: the month is 29 nights, do not fast until you see it, and if obscured, complete 30 days.
This hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim, agreed upon.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'لا تصوموا حتى تروه' (do not fast until you see it) makes sighting a condition. The alternative 'فان غم عليكم فاكملوا العدة' provides the fallback of completing 30 days.
This establishes that sighting is a prerequisite; calculation alone is insufficient.
4
Note the practical application
The commentary criticizes those who rely solely on astronomical birth (neomenia) before actual sighting. The Hanafi position insists on visual sighting or completing 30 days.
This has real-world consequences for moon sighting announcements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۵۵](۵) وينبغي للناس ان يلتمسوا الهلال فى اليوم التاسع والعشرين من شعبان فان راوه صاموا وان غم عليهم اكملوا عدة شعبان ثليثين يوما ثم صاموا . شعبان کی انتیسویں تاریخ کو چاند تلاش کرنا چاہئے۔ اگر نظر آجائے تو روزہ رکھے اور نظر نہ آئے تو شعبان کی تیس پوری کر کے روزہ رکھے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن عبد الله بن عمران رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال الشهر تسع و عشرون ليلة فلا تصوموا حتى تروه فان غم عليكم فاكملوا العدة ثلثين (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب قول النبی اذا رایتم الهلال فصوموا و اذا رایتموه فا فطر وا ص ۲۵۶ نمبر ۱۹۰۷ / مسلم شریف ، باب وجوب صوم رمضان لرویۃ الہلال ص ۳۴۷ نمبر (۱۰۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ چاند دیکھ کر روزہ رکھنا چاہئے اور انتیس کو چاند نظر نہ آئے تو تیس پورے کرے۔ مراکش کو چھوڑ کر عرب کے تقریبا سارے ملک وجود قمر پر یعنی نیومون کے فورا بعد پر کیلنڈر بناتے ہیں جو چاند نظر آنے سے ایک دن مقدم ہوتا ہے۔ اس پر نہ چاند نظر آئے گا اور نہ آ سکتا ہے۔ اللہ تعالی ان کو ہدایت دے۔ برصغیر کے علماء محقق رویت بصری کرتے ہیں اور صحیح تاریخ پر ہمیشہ اعلان کرتے ہیں۔ اللہ ان کو جزائے خیر سے نوازے۔ غم علیکم : چاند چھپ جائے، چاند نظر نہ آئے۔
[555] (5) It is appropriate for people to seek the crescent on the twenty-ninth day of Sha'ban. If they see it, they fast; if it is cloudy/hidden from them, they complete the count of Sha'ban to thirty days, then fast. On the 29th of Sha'ban, the moon should be sought. If it is seen, one fasts; if not seen, complete thirty days of Sha'ban then fast. There is a hadith: from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is twenty-nine nights, so do not fast until you see it; if it is hidden from you, complete the count to thirty.' (Bukhari, Bab qawl al-nabi idha ra'aytum al-hilal fa-sumu wa idha ra'aytumuhu fa-aftiru, p.256 no.1907; Muslim, Bab wujub sawm Ramadan li-ru'yat al-hilal, p.347 no.108) From this hadith it is understood that fasting should begin upon sighting the moon, and if not seen on the 29th, complete thirty days. Except for Morocco, most Arab countries base their calendar on astronomical birth of the moon (neomenia), which precedes actual sighting by a day; so the moon will not be seen and cannot be seen. May Allah guide them. The scholars of the Indian subcontinent verify actual visual sighting and always announce the correct date. May Allah reward them with goodness. 'Ghumma 'alaykum': the moon is hidden, not visible.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for determining the start of Ramadan. It instructs Muslims to look for the new moon on the night of the 29th of Sha'ban. If the crescent is sighted, they begin fasting the next day. If the sky is overcast or the moon is not visible, they must complete 30 days of Sha'ban (i.e., consider Sha'ban a full month of 30 days) and then start Ramadan. The evidence is a hadith from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is twenty-nine nights, so do not fast until you see it; if it is hidden from you, complete the count to thirty.' The commentary adds a practical note: astronomical calculations that claim the moon is born (neomenia) are not sufficient; actual visual sighting is required. The Hanafi school follows sighting, not calculation. The author criticizes those who rely solely on astronomical birth before sighting, as that moon cannot be seen. The term 'ghamma 'alaykum' means the moon is obscured.

Key Terms
الهلال (al-hilal): The new moon crescent that marks the beginning of a lunar month; must be sighted with the naked eye or reliable testimony.
غم عليكم (ghumma 'alaykum): The moon is hidden from view, e.g., by clouds or atmospheric conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this lesson, if the moon is not seen on the 29th of Sha'ban, what should be done?
A) Fast the next day based on calculation
B) Complete 30 days of Sha'ban then fast
C) Seek the moon again the next night
D) Fast two days to be safe

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires actual visual sighting of the crescent or completion of 30 days of Sha'ban to begin Ramadan; astronomical calculation alone is insufficient.

Individual Sight of the Ramadan Crescent
Ruling for individual sighting of Ramadan moon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an individual who alone sights the crescent of Ramadan and apply the principle that personal certainty obligates fasting regardless of the qadi's rejection.

Core Idea
Personal certainty obligates fasting even if the qadi rejects the testimony.

The sighting of the Ramadan crescent is usually a communal event, but what if only one person sees it and the qadi does not accept his testimony? This lesson addresses that exact scenario, clarifying that the individual's own certainty takes precedence over procedural rejection. Let's explore the brief but powerful ruling from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes a person who sees the Ramadan crescent alone and the imam (qadi) does not accept his testimony. Note that the rejection is for procedural reasons, not because the sighting is false.
The qadi may have a policy requiring two witnesses; but the individual is certain.
2
State the ruling
The text explicitly rules: 'Should fast' (ṣāma). The individual must personally observe the fast of Ramadan.
This is independent of the public announcement.
3
Understand the legal basis
The ruling is based on the principle that certainty (yaqīn) overrides doubt. The individual's own certainty of the sighting creates an obligation upon himself, even if his testimony is not accepted by the authority.
Hanafi usul holds that one must act upon one's own certain knowledge.
4
Distinguish from communal ruling
The community does not fast because the qadi's rejection means the crescent is not officially established. But the individual's personal obligation remains.
Thus, the individual fasts privately while the rest do not.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
This principle extends to any situation where a person is certain of a matter that is not accepted by the authorities, such as seeing the Shawwal crescent for Eid.
Hanafi fiqh consistently upholds personal certainty in obligatory acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۵۶](۶) ومن رای الهلال وحده صام وان لم يقبل الامام شهادته . إکيلے آدمی نے رمضان کا چاند دیکھا اور قاضی نے کسی وجہ سے اس کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی تو وہ آدمی خود روزہ رکھ لے۔
[556] (6) Whoever sees the crescent alone should fast, even if the imam (qadi) does not accept his testimony. A single person saw the Ramadan moon and the qadi for some reason did not accept his testimony: that person should himself fast.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This is a brief ruling addressing the situation where a single individual sights the crescent of Ramadan but his testimony is not accepted by the qadi (e.g., due to insufficient witnesses, or the qadi has a policy). In such a case, the individual is obligated to fast personally, even if the general community does not fast. The ruling is based on the principle that certainty for oneself is decisive. The commentary in Urdu clarifies: one person saw the moon, the qadi did not accept his testimony for some reason, but he himself should still fast.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony or witness of sighting the moon; may be rejected by the imam for procedural reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Fast only if he can get another witness
C) Fast himself, because he is certain
D) Wait for the next day's announcement

Key Takeaway
Personal certainty of sighting the Ramadan crescent obligates the individual to fast, regardless of whether the qadi accepts the testimony.

Self-Observed Crescent: Individual Obligation to Fast
Ruling on a person who alone sights the Ramadan crescent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a person who alone sights the Ramadan crescent, explain why kaffarah is not due, and apply the difference between personal certainty and public enforcement.

Core Idea
Personal certainty of the crescent sighting obligates fasting; doubt from judicial rejection prevents kaffarah.

In the Hanafi school, a person who is certain he has seen the Ramadan crescent must act on that certainty, even if the imam rejects his testimony. This lesson explores the ruling, its proof from the hadith, and the important consequence of judicial rejection: while make-up fasting (qada) is required, expiation (kaffarah) is dropped due to a valid doubt (shubha). Understanding this distinction between personal obligation and public enforcement is essential for correctly applying the fiqh of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A person who alone sees the Ramadan crescent. What is the ruling? He must fast (wajib) even if the imam does not accept his testimony.
The ruling is personal, not public.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith 'Fast upon sighting it' (صوموا لرؤيته). Identify the source and transmission level.
This hadith is ahad, making the obligation wajib (not fard) in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'فصوموا' (fast) indicates obligation (wujub) according to the Hanafi principle 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub'.
This connects the dalil to the ruling.
4
Analyze the consequence if omitted
If the person does not fast, what is required? The text says qada (make-up) is obligatory, but no kaffarah (expiation). Why? Because the imam's rejection creates a shubha (doubt), and kaffarah is dropped by shubha.
Shubha here is a legal doubt that averts penalties.
5
Understand the broader principle
The passage concludes with a principle: one may act on his own certainty but cannot impose it on others without a judicial decree or binding testimony.
This separates personal obligation from public enforcement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
هلال رمضان وحده صام وان لم يقبل الامام شهادته
چونکہ وہ آدمی چاند دیکھ چکا ہے اس لئے اس کے حق میں رمضان ہے اس لئے وہ خود روزہ رکھے۔ حدیث میں گزرا کہ چاند دیکھ کر روزہ رکھو اور اس نے چاند دیکھا ہے اس لئے اس کو روزہ رکھنا چاہئے ۔ نون اگر اس نے روزہ نہیں رکھا تو قضا لازم ہوگی کفارہ نہیں۔ کیونکہ قاضی کے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے شبہ پیدا ہو گیا اور کفارہ شبہ سے ساقط ہو جاتا ہے۔
اصول خود کسی بات پر یقین کرتا ہو تو اس کو کرنا چاہئے لیکن دوسروں پر لازم نہیں کر سکتا جب تک کہ قضاء قاضی یا شہادت ملزمہ نہ ہو۔
Book of Fasting. The crescent of Ramadan - if he alone sees it, he fasts, even if the imam does not accept his testimony. Because that person has seen the moon, therefore for him it is Ramadan, so he should himself fast. The hadith has mentioned: 'Fast upon sighting it' and he has sighted it, so he should fast. If he did not fast, then qada (make-up) is obligatory, but no kaffarah, because due to the imam's rejection doubt arises, and kaffarah is dropped by doubt. Principle: If one is certain of something, he should act on it, but he cannot make it obligatory on others unless there is a decree of a qadi or a binding testimony.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on the matn ruling regarding an individual who sight the Ramadan crescent alone. The author begins by stating the ruling: such a person must fast even if the imam (qadi) does not accept his testimony. The explanation follows: because the individual himself is certain of the sighting, the prophetic command 'Fast upon sighting it' applies to him. The hadith is alluded to as already mentioned. If he neglects to fast, qada (make-up) is wajib but kaffarah is not due. The reason is that the imam's rejection creates a valid doubt (shubha) about the obligation, and in Hanafi fiqh, kaffarah penalties are dropped when there is shubha. The passage concludes with a general principle: one may act on his own certainty but cannot impose it on others without a judicial decree or legally binding testimony. This illustrates the Hanafi distinction between personal obligation and public enforcement.

Key Terms
شبہ (shubha): Doubt that affects legal consequences; here, doubt arising from the imam's rejection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person sees the Ramadan crescent alone and the imam rejects his testimony, what happens if he does not fast?
A) He must fast and also give kaffarah.
B) He must make up the fast (qada) but no kaffarah.
C) He is excused because the imam rejected it.
D) He must fast and give charity.

Key Takeaway
Personal certainty of the crescent sighting obligates fasting; doubt from judicial rejection prevents kaffarah.

One Witness for Ramadan, Two for Eid: The Hanafi Rule on Moon Sighting
Ruling on accepting one witness for Ramadan crescent when there is obstruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witness requirements for Ramadan and Eid crescent sighting when there is an obstruction, and distinguish between the two based on the nature of the matter (religious vs transactional).

Core Idea
One trustworthy witness suffices for Ramadan's crescent; two are needed for Eid because it involves leaving an obligation.

Imagine a cloudy evening at the end of Sha'ban. A single trustworthy person comes forward claiming to have seen the new moon. Should the imam accept this testimony and announce the start of Ramadan? The Hanafi school says yes, based on a specific prophetic practice. But if the same happens at the end of Ramadan for Eid, two witnesses are required. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the rationale, and the supporting hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the subject
The text specifies that the ruling applies only when there is an 'illah' – a physical obstruction like clouds, dust, or fog on the horizon. The subject is the imam's obligation regarding witness testimony for the start of Ramadan and for Eid.
The condition (obstruction) is essential; without it, the ruling may differ (though the text does not explicitly cover clear skies).
2
State the ruling for Ramadan
When obstruction exists, the imam accepts the testimony of one trustworthy person (man or woman, free or slave) for sighting the crescent to begin Ramadan. The text calls this a 'religious matter' (amr dini).
The hukm on the imam is wajib – he must accept it.
3
State the ruling for Eid
For the crescent of Eid al-Fitr (and Eid al-Adha, by analogy), two witnesses are required, even if there is an obstruction. The text explains that this is because it involves leaving the fast, which is like a transactional matter requiring two witnesses.
The hadith of two Bedouins is cited as proof for Eid.
4
Analyze the proofs
Two hadith are presented: (1) The hadith of Ibn Abbas: a Bedouin came to the Prophet and said he saw the crescent of Ramadan; the Prophet accepted his testimony and ordered fasting. (2) The hadith of two Bedouins who testified for Eid; the Prophet required two witnesses. The commentator highlights the distinction as the basis for the different rulings.
Both hadith are ahad, giving zanni thubut; they are sufficient for deriving a ruling in non-essential matters.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf and cross-madhhab dispute
The practical consequence: for Ramadan start, one witness is enough; for Eid end, two are required. This Hanafi position is opposed by Maliki and Hanbali schools, who require two for both, and the Shafi'i school, which requires two but may accept one with an oath. The Hanafi evidence is the specific practice of the Prophet.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate; each school relies on different proofs.

واذا كان في السماء علة قبل الامام شهادة الواحد العدل في روية الهلال رجلا كان او امرأة، حرا كان او عبدا.
آسمان میں علت کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ افق پر غبار ہو، کہر ہو یا بادل ہو تو ممکن ہے کہ کسی کو چاند نظر آ جائے اور کسی کو نظر نہ آئے اس لئے ایک آدمی کی گواہی بھی قبول ہوگی۔

If there is an obstruction in the sky, the imam accepts the testimony of one trustworthy person regarding the sighting of the crescent, whether man or woman, free or slave. The meaning of obstruction in the sky is that there is dust, fog, or clouds on the horizon, so it is possible that some see the moon and others not; therefore even one person's testimony is accepted. The matter of moon sighting is a religious matter (amr dini), not transactions, so one person's testimony is acceptable; there is no need for two witnesses. Hadith: From Rib'i bin Hirash from a man of the Companions of the Prophet said: The people differed on the last day of Ramadan, so two Bedouins came and testified before the Prophet by Allah that they saw the crescent last evening, so the Messenger of Allah commanded the people to break the fast. (Abu Dawud) This hadith indicates that for Eid al-Fitr crescent, two witnesses are necessary. In Dar Qutni: They said: The Prophet would not accept testimony for breaking the fast except by the testimony of two men. (Dar Qutni) This hadith indicates that if the sky has obstruction, for Eid, judgement is made by two witnesses, not less. Because the obligation to leave fasting depends on it, and it involves people's benefit, so it becomes like transactions, and transactions require two witnesses. Therefore, for the crescent of Eid and Bakrid, two witnesses are needed. For starting the fast of Ramadan, one witness is needed because it is a religious matter, and for establishing a religious matter one witness is sufficient. Hadith: From Ibn Abbas: A Bedouin came to the Prophet and said: I saw the crescent. (Al-Hasan said in his hadith: 'of Ramadan.') He said: Do you testify that there is no god but Allah? He said: Yes. He said: Do you testify that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah? He said: Yes. He said: O Bilal, announce to the people that they should fast tomorrow. (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi) Footnotes: (a) The people differed on the last day of Ramadan, so two Bedouins came and testified before the Prophet by Allah that they saw the moon last evening, so the Prophet commanded the people to break the fast. (b) He would not accept testimony for breaking the fast except by two witnesses. (c) A Bedouin (continues on next page)
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for when there is an obstruction (علة) such as clouds or dust. The matn states that in such a case the imam accepts the testimony of one trustworthy person, regardless of gender or status. The commentator explains why: moon sighting is a religious matter (amr dini), not a transactional matter, so one witness suffices. Two hadiths are cited to differentiate between Ramadan and Eid. For Eid, two witnesses are needed because it involves the obligation of breaking the fast, which is a communal matter analogous to transactions. The hadith of the two Bedouins shows that for Eid al-Fitr, the Prophet required two witnesses. For Ramadan, the hadith of Ibn Abbas shows that one witness is enough for starting the fast. The commentator emphasizes that for Ramadan, it is a religious act of worship, and one witness suffices based on the practice of the Prophet.

Key Terms
علة (illah): Natural obstruction on the horizon such as clouds, dust, or fog that may hide the moon.
أمر ديني (amr dini): Religious matter, as opposed to transactional matters, which allows for greater leniency in testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many witnesses are required for testifying to the crescent of Eid al-Fitr when there is an obstruction?
A) One witness, like Ramadan.
B) Two witnesses, because it involves leaving an obligation and is like a transactional matter.
C) Four witnesses.
D) Women are not accepted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts one trustworthy witness for the Ramadan crescent if there is an obstruction, but requires two witnesses for the Eid crescent, based on hadith evidence and the nature of the matter (religious vs transactional).

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Clear Sky Requires Mass Sighting; Time of Fast
Ruling on mass sighting in clear sky; time of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on moon sighting when the sky is clear, explain the evidential basis (dalil) for requiring mass sighting, and identify the time of fasting from fajr thani to sunset.

Core Idea
In clear sky, mass sighting is required for Ramadan; individual testimony is rejected.

This lesson addresses two fundamental fiqh rulings related to Ramadan: the method of establishing the crescent when the sky is clear, and the precise time of fasting. The Hanafi school applies a principle of certainty (yaqin) to moon sighting, requiring widespread testimony. The time of fast is explicitly defined by the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Distinct Rulings
The passage covers two separate issues: (1) the evidentiary requirement for crescent sighting in clear sky, and (2) the time bracket for fasting. Distinguish these clearly.
2
Understand the Reasoning for Mass Sighting
When the sky is clear, the moon should be visible to many. A claim of sighting by only one or two individuals is contrary to the norm ('ādah) and thus rejected. The goal is certainty (yaqin).
The athar from Ata' reinforces this: a person should not act on a private sighting if the public did not see it.
3
Examine the Athar from Ata'
The passage cites an athar where Ata' prohibits fasting or breaking fast based on a private sighting one night before the public. This supports requiring mass sighting.
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
4
Derive the Time from the Quran
The time of fasting is from the true dawn (fajr thani) until sunset. This is derived directly from Quran 2:187, which mentions the white and black thread of dawn.
The verse is qat'i in thubut and dalalah.

Source Text

فان لم يكن فى السماء علة لم تقبل الشهادة حتى يراه جمع كثير يقع العلم بخبرهم [۵۵۹] (۹) ووقت الصوم من حين طلوع الفجر الثاني الى غروب الشمس
اور اگر آسمان میں علت نہ ہو تو گواہی قبول نہیں کی جائے گی یہاں تک کہ ایک بڑی جماعت دیکھے جس کی خبر سے علم یقینی واقع ہو اگر آسمان پر بادل، غبار، کہر ، دھواں وغیرہ نہیں ہے اور چاند نظر آنے کے قابل ہو گیا ہے تو ہر ڈھونڈنے والے کو نظر آئے گا اور کافی آدمی اس کو دیکھیں گے ۔ لیکن اس کے باوجود ایک دو آدمیوں نے دیکھنے کا دعوی کیا تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ وہ جھوٹ بول رہے ہیں۔ اور محال عادی ہے اس لئے ایک دو آدمیوں کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جائے گی۔ بلکہ اتنے آدمی دیکھیں کہ اس کی خبر سے علم یقینی ہو اور جھوٹ پر محمول نہ کیا جا سکے۔ اثر میں ہے قلت لعطاء ارایت لو ان رجلا رای هلال رمضان قبل الناس بليلة ايصوم قبلهم ويفطر قبلهم ؟ قال لا الا ان راه الناس اخشى يكون شبه عليه (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب کم یجوز من الشهو وعلی رویۃ المصلال ج رابع ص ۶۷ نمبر ۷۳۴۸ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ رویت عامہ ہو تب قبول کیا جائے گا۔ زمانے کا تجربہ یہ ہے کہ جب چاند دیکھنے کے قابل ہو جاتا ہے اور مطلع صاف ہو تو ہر آدمی کو نظر آتا ہے۔ لیکن دکھنے کے قابل نہ ہو تو کسی تجربه کو نظر نہیں آتا۔ ایسے موقع پر ایک دو گواہی گزرتی ہے اور وہ جھوٹی گواہی ہوتی ہے۔ اس وقت چاند آسمان پر ہلال ہی بنا نہیں ہوتا۔ چاند نیومون سے اٹھارہ گھنٹے کے بعد دیکھنے کے قابل ہوتا ہے۔ جو لوگ اس سے قبل دیکھنے کا دعوی کرتے ہیں وہ جھوٹ بولتے ہیں۔ و عرب کے علماء نے ایک گواہی اور دو گواہی پر چاند ہونے کا فیصلہ دیا اور رویت عامہ کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جس کا نتیجہ یہ ہوا کہ وہاں کیلنڈر ایک دن مقدم اور دیڑھ دن مقدم تاریخ پر بنائی گئی۔ اور اسی پر ایک دو گواہی لیکر رویت کا اعلان کرتے ہیں۔ اور ساڑھے ستائیس پر یا اٹھائیس پر گواہی لیتے ہیں۔ اور اعلان رویت کر لیتے ہیں ان کا کبھی بھی حقیقت میں انتیس اور تمہیں پورے نہیں ہوتے۔ صرف مقدم کیلنڈر کا انتیس اور تمیں پورا کرتے ہیں جو قطعا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اور ایک روز فرض روزہ ضائع کرتے ہیں۔ العیاذ والحفیظ! اس لئے مطلع صاف ہو تو رویت عامہ پر رویت کا فیصلہ کرنا چاہئے۔ (۵۵۹] (۹) روزہ کا وقت صبح صادق طلوع ہونے کی وقت سے سورج غروب ہونے تک ہے۔ صبح صادق کے وقت سے لیکر غروب آفتاب تک روزہ کا وقت ہے۔
آیت میں ہے وكلوا واشربوا حتى تيبين لكم الخيط الابيض من الخيط الاسود من الفجر ثم اتموا الصيام الى الليل
If there is no obstruction in the sky, the testimony is not accepted until a large group sees it, whose report produces certainty. And if there is no obstruction and the moon becomes visible, then every seeker will see it and enough people will see it. But despite that, if one or two people claim to have seen it, that means they are lying, and it is contrary to the norm, so their testimony is not accepted. Rather, so many people must see that their report produces certainty and cannot be attributed to falsehood. There is an athar: I said to Ata: What if a man saw the Ramadan crescent a night before the people, should he fast before them and break before them? He said: No, unless the people see it; I fear he might be mistaken. (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) This athar indicates that when there is a general sighting, it is accepted. The experience of time is that when the moon becomes visible and the sky is clear, every person sees it. But if it is not visible, no one of experience sees it. At such a time, one or two testimonies occur, and they are false. At that time, the moon has not formed as a crescent. The moon becomes visible eighteen hours after the new moon. Those who claim to see it before that are lying. The scholars of Arabia have given verdicts on one or two testimonies and did not consider general sighting, the result being that their calendar was made one day or one and a half days early. And they declare sighting based on one or two testimonies. They take testimony on the 27th or 28th and declare sighting, but they never complete 29 or 30 days in reality. They only complete 29 or 30 days in the advanced calendar, which is absolutely not permissible. And they waste one day of obligatory fast. Therefore, when the sky is clear, the sighting should be decided based on general sighting. (9) The time of fasting is from the rising of the true dawn (fajr sadiq) until sunset. The verse says: 'And eat and drink until the white thread becomes distinct to you from the black thread of dawn, then complete the fast until the night.'
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage covers two separate rulings. First: when the sky is clear (no obstruction), the testimony of one or two is not accepted; rather, a large group must see the moon to produce certainty. The commentator explains the reasoning: if the moon is visible, many would see it; so a claimed sighting by only a few is a sign of falsehood. He supports this with an athar from Ata: one cannot act on a private sighting if others did not see it. He criticizes the practice of some scholars who accept one or two testimonies even when the sky is clear, resulting in an early calendar and missing a day of fasting. The relied-upon Hanafi ruling is that when the sky is clear, general sighting (ru'yah 'ammah) is required. Second ruling: the time of fasting is from true dawn (fajr sadiq) until sunset, as per the Quranic verse about the white and black thread.

Key Terms
رویت عامہ (ru'yah 'ammah): General sighting by many people that produces certainty.
فجر ثانی (fajr thani): The true dawn (second dawn) which is the beginning of the fast.
محال عادی (muhal 'adi): Something normally impossible; goes against established norm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in clear sky, when is the testimony of one or two people regarding the crescent accepted?
A) Always accepted.
B) Accepted only if they are righteous.
C) Not accepted; mass sighting is required.
D) Accepted only if they give oath.

Key Takeaway
In clear sky, the Hanafi school requires mass sighting for certainty; the time of fasting is from true dawn to sunset, as per Quran 2:187.

Moon Sighting Testimony in Clear Skies
Ruling on testimony for Ramadan moon when sky is clear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting moon sighting testimony in clear skies and explain the rational and transmitted evidence behind it.

Core Idea
In clear skies, a single witness for moon sighting is rejected — only a crowd producing certain knowledge suffices.

This lesson addresses a critical issue in moon sighting: when the sky is clear without obstruction, the Hanafi school requires widespread sighting (tawatur) to accept the testimony. The author of Al-Sharh al-Samiri explains that if only one or two people claim to see the moon in such conditions, it is likely false. We will explore the reasoning, the transmitted evidence from 'Ata, and the practical consequence of rejecting weak testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
When the sky is clear (no clouds, dust, fog, etc.), the passage states that testimony of one or two witnesses is not accepted until a large crowd sees it.
The Arabic term for obstruction is 'illah, used in a non-technical sense.
2
Understand the role of 'illah
The presence of an obstruction (clouds etc.) is the condition that allows acceptance of limited testimony. Its absence triggers the stricter rule.
The 'illah here is a physical obstruction, not the usuli term for effective cause.
3
Analyze the rational proof
If the sky is clear and the moon is visible, everyone would see it. Therefore, a claim by few suggests falsehood. This is a rational demonstration based on habitual impossibility.
This is an example of using rational reasoning (logical impossibility) as a proof.
4
Examine the dalil naqli
The athar from 'Ata ibn Abi Rabah shows that even a person who sees the moon a night before others should not act alone; only general sighting is acceptable.
The athar is found in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq and supports the requirement of general sighting.
5
Compare with opposing practice
Some scholars accept one or two testimonies even in clear skies, but the Hanafi view rejects this as dangerous, leading to loss of an obligatory fast.
The author strongly condemns this practice as absolutely impermissible.

(8) If there is no obstruction in the sky, testimony is not accepted until a large crowd sees it whose report gives certain knowledge. (9) The time of fasting is from the rising of the second dawn to sunset. From this hadith it is known that for establishing Ramadan, the testimony of one person is sufficient. (8) And if there is no obstruction in the sky, testimony will not be accepted until a large group sees it from whose report certain knowledge occurs. If there are no clouds, dust, fog, smoke, etc. in the sky and the moon has become visible, then every seeker will see it and enough people will see it. But despite this, if one or two people claim to have seen it, it means they are lying. And it is habitually impossible, so the testimony of one or two people will not be accepted. Rather, so many people must see that from their report certain knowledge occurs and it cannot be attributed to lying. There is an athar: I said to 'Ata: 'What do you think if a man sees the crescent of Ramadan a night before the people? Should he fast before them and break fast before them?' He said: 'No, unless the people see it. I fear it may be a confusion for him.' (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, باب کم یجوز من الشهو وعلی رویۃ المصلال، vol. 4, p. 67, no. 7348) From this athar it is known that only when there is general sighting will it be accepted. The experience of time is that when the moon becomes visible and the sky is clear, every person sees it. But if it is not visible, no one sees it. At such a time, one or two testimonies pass and they are false testimony. At that time, the moon has not even formed as a crescent on the sky. The moon becomes visible eighteen hours after the new moon. Those who claim to see it before that are lying. The scholars of Arabia have given a ruling on the moon based on one or two testimonies and did not consider general sighting, resulting in the calendar being set one day or one and a half days ahead. And on this, they announce sighting based on one or two testimonies. They take testimony on the 27th or 28th and announce sighting. They never actually complete 29 or 30 days in reality. They only complete 29 or 30 days on the advanced calendar, which is absolutely not permissible. And they waste one obligatory fast. We seek refuge with Allah! Therefore, if the sky is clear, the ruling on sighting should be based on general sighting.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on accepting testimony for the sighting of the Ramadan crescent when the sky is clear (no obstruction). The author begins by stating the main ruling: if there is no obstruction in the sky (e.g., clouds, dust, fog, smoke), the testimony of one or two witnesses is not accepted; rather, a large crowd must see it such that their report yields certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini). The reasoning is that when the sky is clear and the moon is visible, everyone would see it, so if only one or two claim to have seen it, they are likely lying (hilf). The author then provides an athar from 'Ata ibn Abi Rabah, who was asked about a person who sees the moon a night before the general public. 'Ata responded that he should not fast or break fast separately; rather, the sighting must be general (ru'yat 'ammah) to avoid confusion. The author then criticizes the practice of some Arab scholars who accept single or dual testimony even when the sky is clear, leading to calendar inaccuracies and wasting a day of obligatory fast. The Hanafi position is that when the sky is clear, only widespread testimony (tawatur) suffices, not individual testimony. This is based on the principle of blocking the means to falsehood (sadd al-dhara'i') and the priority of certainty over doubt.

Key Terms
علة ('illah): Here, 'illah means obstruction in the sky such as clouds, dust, fog, smoke that prevents clear sighting.
علم یقینی (ilm yaqini): Certain knowledge that eliminates doubt; here, from a report of a large crowd (tawatur).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling presented, what is required for moon sighting when the sky is clear?
A) One trustworthy witness
B) Two male witnesses
C) A large crowd that produces certain knowledge
D) The testimony of a woman

Key Takeaway
In clear weather, only widespread testimony (tawatur) yields certain knowledge and is accepted; a small number of witnesses are considered unreliable.

Temporal Boundaries of the Fast: From True Dawn to Sunset
Ruling on the time period for fasting with Quranic and hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the start and end times of the daily fast according to the Hanafi school and identify the Quranic and hadith proofs that establish these times.

Core Idea
The fast begins at true dawn (fajr sadiq) and ends at sunset — clear texts remove all doubt.

Knowing precisely when to start and end the fast is essential for its validity. The classical Hanafi text defines the temporal boundary with Quranic and prophetic authority. In this lesson, we will examine the two key texts: the verse of the white thread and the hadiths that delineate true dawn from false dawn and sunset from nightfall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The text states that the time of fasting is from the rising of the second dawn to sunset. This is a ruling (hukm) establishing the obligatory period for the fast.
The second dawn (al-fajr al-thani) is the true dawn.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The author quotes Surah al-Baqarah 2:187. Locate the imperative 'atimmu al-siyam' (complete the fast) and the temporal phrase 'until the white thread becomes distinct...'. This sets the start at true dawn and the end at nightfall.
The 'white thread' is interpreted by the Hanafi school as the light of true dawn.
3
Analyze the hadith proof for the start
The hadith of Samurah warns against being deceived by Bilal's call or the early whiteness (false dawn). The key phrase 'until it spreads widely' (yastatir) indicates true dawn. This refines the start time.
False dawn (fajr awwal) is a vertical light; true dawn spreads horizontally.
4
Analyze the hadith proof for the end
The hadith of 'Umar defines sunset: when night comes from the east, day retreats from the west, and the sun sets, then the fasting person breaks the fast.
Sunset is determined by the disappearance of the solar disk below the horizon.
5
Synthesize the proofs into the ruling
Both Quran and Sunnah converge: the fast begins at true dawn and ends at sunset. There is no ikhtilaf on this among the schools; it is muttafaq alayh.
This ruling is fard (obligatory) to observe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووقت الصوم من حين طلوع الفجر الثاني الى غروب الشمس آیت میں ہے وكلوا واشربوا حتى تيبين لكم الخيط الابيض من الخيط الاسود من الفجر ثم اتموا الصيام الى الشمس [۵۶۰] (۱۰) والصوم هو الامساك عن الاكل والشرب والجماع نهارا مع النية حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے) آیت (۱۸۷ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ صبح صادق کے پہلے پہلے تک کھاتا رہے گا اور صبح صادق کے وقت سے روزہ شروع ہو گا اور غروب آفتاب تک رہے گا۔ خیط ابیض سے مراد صبح صادق ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے سمرة بن جندب يقول سمعت محمدا لله يقول لا يغرن احدكم نداء بلال من السحور ولا هذا البياض حتى يستطير (ب) (مسلم شریف، باب ان الدخول في الصوم يحصل بطلوع الفجر ص ۳۵۰ نمبر ۱۰۹۴ر بخاری شریف نمبر ۱۹۱۷ / ابو داؤد شریف، باب وقت السحور ۳۴۱ نمبر ۲۳۴۶) اس حدیث سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ صبح صادق سے روزہ شروع ہوگا۔ عن عمر ابن خطاب قال قال رسول الله اذا اقبل الليل من ههنا وادبر النهار من ههنا وغربت الشمس فقد افطر الصائم (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب متی محل فطر الصائم ص ۲۶۲ نمبر ۱۹۵۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ آفتاب غروب ہونے کے بعد روز و افطار کرے۔
And the time of fasting is from the rising of the second dawn to sunset. In the verse: 'And eat and drink until the white thread becomes distinct to you from the black thread of dawn; then complete the fast until night' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187). This verse indicates that you may eat until just before true dawn, and fasting begins at true dawn and continues until sunset. 'White thread' means true dawn. In the hadith: Samurah ibn Jundub said: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: 'Let not the call of Bilal nor this whiteness deceive you for the suhur until it spreads widely.' (Muslim, no. 1094; Bukhari, no. 1917; Abu Dawud, no. 2346). This hadith also indicates that fasting begins at true dawn. From 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, the Messenger of Allah said: 'When night comes from here and day goes from here and the sun has set, then the fasting person breaks his fast.' (Bukhari, no. 1954). This hadith indicates that after sunset, the fasting person breaks his fast.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal boundaries of the fast. The author first states the time: from the rising of the second dawn (al-fajr al-thani, i.e., true dawn) to sunset. He then provides textual evidence: the Quranic verse (2:187) which speaks of the white thread becoming distinct from the black thread at dawn, and the command to complete the fast until night. The commentary explains that white thread refers to true dawn (al-fajr al-sadiq), which is the light spreading horizontally across the horizon. The hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub warns against being deceived by Bilal's call or the false dawn (vertical light) for suhur; true dawn is when the light spreads widely (yastatir). Another hadith from 'Umar establishes that after sunset (when night comes and day departs), the fasting person breaks his fast. Thus, the time for fasting is precisely from the appearance of true dawn until complete sunset. The passage also mentions that definition of fasting (imsak) is reserved for the next section.

Key Terms
الفجر الثاني (al-fajr al-thani): The second dawn, i.e., true dawn (al-fajr al-sadiq), which is the light spreading horizontally on the horizon.
خيط ابيض (khayt abyad): White thread, a metaphor for the true dawn (light).
يستطير (yastatir): It spreads widely (referring to the light of true dawn spreading across the horizon).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When Bilal gives the adhan
B) When the false dawn appears
C) When the whiteness spreads widely (yastatir)
D) At the middle of the night

Key Takeaway
The fast is bounded by two clear signs from revelation: the spreading light of true dawn and the setting of the sun.

The Hanafi Definition of Fasting
Definition of the act of fasting with its essential elements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting and identify its essential components: abstention from three acts and intention.

Core Idea
Fasting is abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse with intention during daylight.

The author presents a concise definition of fasting (sawm) that captures its legal essence in the Hanafi school. This definition is not merely linguistic but specifies the actions from which one must abstain and the requirement of intention. Understanding this definition is foundational for all rulings on fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being defined
What is being defined? The term 'al-sawm' (fasting). Note that it is a legal (shar'i) definition, not merely linguistic.
2
List the elements of abstention (imsak)
From what must one abstain? The text specifies three things: eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse (jima'). These are the nullifiers of the fast.
Other schools may include additional nullifiers, but the Hanafi school limits to these three.
3
Note the time condition
The abstention must be 'during the day' (naharan). This excludes nighttime, when eating, drinking, and marital relations are permitted.
The day begins at true dawn (fajr sadiq) and ends at sunset.
4
Recognize the role of intention (niyyah)
The definition includes 'with intention' (ma'a al-niyyah). Intention is a condition for the fast to be valid; mere abstention without intention is not considered fasting.
Intention must be made before dawn for obligatory fasts.
5
Distinguish between linguistic and legal meanings
Linguistically, 'sawm' means any abstention. Legally, it is restricted to specific acts during the day with intention. This distinction is important for correct application.
The definition is an example of 'definition by specifying the essential elements' (hadd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۵۶۰] (۱۰) والصوم هو الامساك عن الاكل والشرب والجماع نهارا مع النية
(10) And fasting is abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse during the day with intention.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
The author provides a concise definition of fasting (sawm). It consists of three essential components: abstention (imsak), from three specific acts: eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse (jima'), during the daytime (naharan), and with intention (niyyah). Intention is a condition for validity; without it, the abstention is not considered fasting. This definition is standard in the Hanafi school. The commentary earlier on page 358 had already mentioned the time period, so the definition complements that.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Linguistically: abstention; legally: specific abstention with intention during the day.
الامساك (al-imsak): Abstention, holding back from the nullifiers of fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fasting is precise: abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention; intention is a condition for validity.

Forgetfulness Does Not Invalidate the Fast
Ruling that unintentional eating/drinking/jima' does not break the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating/drinking/jima' during a fast and explain the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness does not break the fast – the act is forgiven and the fast remains valid.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a fasting person unintentionally eats, drinks, or has sexual intercourse due to forgetfulness (nisyan). Many assume the fast is invalidated, but the Hanafi school, supported by authentic hadith, holds that the fast continues and no compensation (qada or kaffarah) is required. Let's examine the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating, drinking, or jima' forgetfully (nasiyan). The ruling: the fast is not broken (lam yuftir), it remains valid (sahih).
The ruling explicitly covers all three actions.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith are cited: the first from Bukhari and Abu Dawud, the second from Dar Qutni.
Both are ahad hadith, but accepted as evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In the first hadith, the command 'fal-yutimma sawmahu' (let him complete his fast) implies the fast is still valid. The phrase 'innama at'amahu Allah wa saqahu' shows it was divine provision, not a sin. In the second hadith, explicit negation of qada and kaffarah.
The second hadith is explicit (qati al-dalala) on the negation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Most schools agree, but some (e.g., some Malikis) require qada. Hanafi: no qada.
Hanafi position is based on the explicit negation.
5
Consider the inclusion of jima'
The hadith mention only eating and drinking; jima' is included by analogy (qiyas) because the cause (forgetfulness) is the same.
This is a standard Hanafi extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۵۶۱) (۱۱) فان اكل الصائم او شرب او جامع ناسيا لم يفطر بھول کر کھانے پینے اور جماع کرنے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ کیونکہ بھول کرنا وغیرہ معاف ہے۔ عن ابی ھریرة عن النبی الله قال اذا اکل او شرب فليتم صومه فانما اطعمه الله وسقاه (د) (بخاری شریف، باب الصائم اذا اکل او شرب ناسیا ص ۲۵۹ نمبر ۱۹۳۳ را بو داؤد شریف، باب من اکل ناسیاص ۳۳۳ نمبر ۲۳۹۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ بھول سے کھایا یا پیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹا اس کو پورا کرے (۲) عن ابي هريرة عن النبي عل الله قال من افطر في شهر رمضان ناسيا فلا قضاء عليه ولا كفارة وليتم صومه (ه) (دار قطنی ۳ کتاب الصوم، ج ثانی ص ۱۵۸ نمبر ۲۲۲۳) اس حدیث سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بھول سے کھایا پیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹا اور نہ اس کی قضا کرنے کی ضرورت ہے۔ اور نہ کفارہ دینے کی ضرورت ہے۔
(11) If the fasting person eats, drinks, or has sexual intercourse forgetfully, he does not break his fast. Forgetfully eating, drinking, or having intercourse does not break the fast because forgetfulness and similar things are forgiven. From Abu Huraira from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'If he eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for indeed Allah has fed him and given him drink.' (Bukhari, no. 1933; Abu Dawud, no. 2398). This hadith indicates that if one eats or drinks forgetfully, the fast is not broken; he should complete it. (2) From Abu Huraira from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no make-up (qada') upon him and no expiation; let him complete his fast.' (Dar Qutni, Kitab al-Sawm, vol. 2, p. 158, no. 2223). This hadith also indicates that if one eats or drinks forgetfully, the fast is not broken, nor is there any need to make it up or give expiation.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a fasting person unintentionally eats, drinks, or engages in sexual intercourse due to forgetfulness (nisyan). The author states clearly that such actions do not invalidate the fast. The reason given is that forgetfulness is forgiven (ma'af). Two authentic hadith are cited: first, the Prophet said that if one eats or drinks forgetfully, he should complete his fast because Allah fed him and gave him drink (i.e., it was a provision from Allah). The second hadith explicitly states that no qada (make-up) or kaffarah (expiation) is required. The Hanafi school follows these hadith and considers the fast valid. This is a concession from Allah for human forgetfulness. The commentary also implies that sexual intercourse forgetfully has the same ruling, though the hadith only mentions eating and drinking; by analogy (qiyas), the Hanafi school includes it.

Key Terms
ناسیا (nasiyan): Forgetfully, unintentionally, without awareness of being in a state of fasting.
قضاء (qada): To make up a missed fast on another day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Break the fast and make it up later
B) Continue fasting, no make-up required
C) Break the fast and offer expiation
D) Continue fasting but make it up later

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness does not break the fast, and no make-up or expiation is required.

Actions That Do Not Break the Fast (Hanafi Rulings)
List of actions that do not break the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six actions listed by the author that do not invalidate the fast and explain the Hanafi principle behind their permissibility.

Core Idea
Involuntary emissions and external applications do not break the fast.

Fasting requires abstinence from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset. However, not every bodily event or external application nullifies the fast. This passage enumerates specific actions that, despite potential emission or nourishment, leave the fast valid according to the Hanafi school. Understanding these exceptions is essential for correct practice and avoiding unnecessary invalidation.

Guided Path
1
List the actions
Identify each of the six actions mentioned: sleeping with nocturnal emission, looking at a woman leading to ejaculation, applying oil, cupping, applying kohl, and kissing.
The text is cut off after 'kissing'; other actions may follow.
2
Classify each action as voluntary or involuntary
Determine whether the action is voluntary (e.g., applying oil) or involuntary (e.g., nocturnal emission). Involuntary actions never break the fast; voluntary ones are valid only if they do not involve direct ingestion or deliberate sexual release.
Kissing is voluntary but does not break the fast unless it leads to ejaculation or intercourse.
3
Identify the underlying Hanafi principle
The common thread: none of these actions involve the direct entry of a substance into the body through the mouth or private parts, and they do not constitute deliberate sexual intercourse. Therefore, they do not nullify the fast.
This is based on istihsan and specific hadith evidence.
4
Compare with other schools
Note that some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider cupping or kohl as breakers. The Hanafi school disagrees, relying on evidence that these actions do not constitute eating or drinking.
This ikhtilaf has practical implications for observing the fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۵۶۲] (۱۲) اگر سو گیا اور احتلام ہوا (۲) یا عورت کی طرف دیکھا اور انزال ہوا (۳) یا تیل لگایا (۴) یا پچھنا لگایا (۵) یا سرمہ لگایا (۶) یا بوسہ
(12) If he slept and had a nocturnal emission, or looked at a woman and ejaculated, or applied oil, or cupped, or applied kohl, or kissed...
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage lists several actions that, despite potentially causing emission or affecting the body, do not break the fast according to the Hanafi school. The list includes: 1) sleeping and having a wet dream (ihtilam) – since it is involuntary; 2) looking at a woman and ejaculating (inzal) – if it is without direct contact and unintended; 3) applying oil to the body or hair; 4) cupping (hijama); 5) applying kohl (surma) to the eyes; 6) kissing (bosa) – provided it does not lead to actual intercourse or deliberate ejaculation. The text is cut off but the pattern is clear: these actions are considered non-nullifying based on the principle that they do not involve direct entry of something into the body through the mouth or private parts, or they are involuntary. The Hanafi school relies on istihsan and hadith evidence for each. The author does not provide proofs here but merely states the rulings.

Key Terms
احتلام (ihtilam): Nocturnal emission, involuntary discharge of semen during sleep.
انزال (inzal): Ejaculation, here from looking, not from direct sexual contact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Drinking intentionally
C) Applying kohl
D) Having sexual intercourse deliberately

Key Takeaway
Involuntary emissions and external applications (oil, cupping, kohl, kissing without emission) do not break the fast according to the Hanafi school.

Forgetful Acts During Fasting: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling with proof from two hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating/drinking/sexual intercourse during fasting and identify the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness erases culpability: forgetful acts do not break the fast.

This passage addresses a common concern: what if a fasting person eats or drinks out of forgetfulness? The Hanafi school, drawing on clear prophetic hadith, rules that the fast remains valid and no makeup or expiation is required. We will examine the textual evidence and the legal reasoning behind this lenient ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What acts does the passage address? What is the ruling on the fast?
2
Locate the dalil
Which hadith are cited? Note their source and key wording.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How do the hadith prove the ruling? Identify the key phrase that indicates validity.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Are there any schools that disagree? What is their basis?
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the Hanafi position and the opposing view?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۶۱] (۱۱) فان اكل الصائم او شرب او جامع ناسيا لم يفطر پس اگر روزہ دار نے کھانا کھایا یا پیا یا جماع کیا بھول کر تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ بھول کر کھانے پینے اور جماع کرنے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ کیونکہ بھول کرنا وغیرہ معاف ہے۔ عن ابی ھریرة عن النبی الله قال اذا فاكل او شرب فليتم صومه فانما اطعمه الله وسقاه (د) ( بخاری شریف، باب الصائم اذا اكل او شرب ناسیا ص ۲۵۹ نمبر ۱۹۳۳ را بو داؤد شریف، باب من اكل ناسیاص ۳۳۳ نمبر ۲۳۹۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کہ بھول سے کھایا یا پیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹا اس کو پورا کرے (۲) عن ابي هريرة عن النبي عل الله قال من افطر في شهر رمضان ناسيا فلا قضاء عليه ولا كفارة وليتم صومه (ه) (دار قطنی ۳ کتاب الصوم، ج ثانی ص ۱۵۸ نمبر ۲۲۲۳) اس حدیث سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بھول سے کھایا پیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹا اور نہ اس کی قضا کرنے کی ضرورت ہے۔ اور نہ کفارہ دینے کی ضرورت ہے۔
So if a fasting person ate or drank or had intercourse forgetfully, he does not break his fast. ...
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from two hadith. The legal ruling is that forgetful eating, drinking, or sexual intercourse does not invalidate the fast. The first hadith (from Bukhari) states that one should complete the fast regardless, as Allah provided the food and drink. The second hadith (from Darqutni) clarifies that no qada (makeup) and no kaffarah (expiation) are required. In the Hanafi school, this is the relied-upon position. Note that deliberate violation requires both qada and kaffarah.

Key Terms
ناسيا (nasiyan): In a state of forgetfulness; unintentionally, without remembrance of the fast.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed fast on another day.
كفارة (kaffarah): An expiatory penalty for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is broken and must be made up with expiation
B) The fast is broken but only a makeup fast is required
C) The fast remains valid and no makeup or expiation is needed
D) The fast becomes invalid and the day must be repeated twice

Key Takeaway
Forgetful consumption during fasting does not break the fast; the fast remains valid and no compensation (qada or kaffarah) is required.
