Wet Medicine and the Fast: When Does It Break?
Ruling on wet medicine reaching interior breaking fast, with explanation of wet vs dry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on applying wet medicine to a wound during fasting and identify the condition that triggers invalidity.

Core Idea
Wet medicine that certainly reaches the stomach or brain invalidates the fast; dry medicine does not.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case: a person applies medicine to a wound while fasting. The ruling depends on whether the medicine is wet or dry, and whether it actually reaches the interior (stomach or brain). We will explore the Hanafi position and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is applying wet medicine to a wound. The ruling: if it reaches the interior, the fast is broken.
2
Distinguish wet vs dry
Wet medicine can flow; dry medicine does not reach the interior. Only wet medicine can break the fast.
3
Note the condition: certainty of reaching
The fast breaks only if the wet medicine certainly reaches the stomach or brain. If uncertain, the fast remains intact.
This condition is key for the dispute with Sahibayn.
4
Understand the immediate effects
When the fast is broken, qada is required on another day, but the current day must still be completed by abstaining until sunset.
This is a Hanafi principle for invalidated fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدواء رطب فوصل الى جوفه او دماغه افطر
اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کوئی چیز بدن میں داخل کرنے سے دوسرے دن روزہ قضا ر کھے۔ البتہ اس دن بھی روزہ پورا کرے چھوڑے نہیں۔ ات الفن : پاخانہ کے راستے سے دوا پیٹ میں ڈالنا۔ آمنہ : دماغ کا گہراز ثم جو دماغ کے اندر تک پہنچ رہا ہو۔ رطب : تر۔ تر دوا کی قید اس لئے لگائی کہ تر دو از خم کی رطوبت کے ساتھ مل کر پیٹ یا دماغ تک پہنچ جاتی ہے۔ جب کہ خشک دوا زخم کی رطوبت کو اور مزید خشک کر دیتی ہے اس لئے وہ آنت تک نہیں پہنچ پاتی۔ اس لئے خشک کے لگانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
نا صاحبین کے نزدیک تر دو الگانے سے بھی روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا کیونکہ آنت تک پہنچنا اور دماغ تک پہنچنا کوئی یقینی نہیں ہے۔ اصول دوایا غذاد ماغ یا پیٹ تک پہنچ جائے تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔
With wet medicine that reached his interior or his brain, he breaks fast. From the effect it is known that from something entering the body, the next day he should make up the fast. However, that day he should complete the fast, do not leave it. Al-Fan: inserting medicine into the stomach through the anus. Aamina: the deep part of the brain that reaches the inside of the brain. Ratb: wet. The condition of wet medicine is because wet medicine mixes with the moisture of the wound and reaches the stomach or brain. Whereas dry medicine further dries the wound's moisture, so it does not reach the intestine. Therefore, applying dry medicine does not break the fast. According to the Sahibayn, applying wet medicine also does not break the fast because reaching the intestine and reaching the brain is not certain. The principle: if a medicine or food reaches the stomach or brain, the fast breaks.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn along with explanatory commentary. The ruling is: if a person applies wet medicine to a wound and it reaches the interior of the body (stomach or brain), the fast is broken. The commentary clarifies that 'wet medicine' here refers to liquid preparations that can travel through the wound into the body. Dry medicine, conversely, absorbs wound moisture and does not penetrate to the intestine, so it does not break the fast. The commentary also records a dispute: according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), even wet medicine does not break the fast because there is no certainty that it actually reaches the interior. However, the principle stated at the end affirms that if any substance certainly reaches the stomach or brain, the fast is broken.

Key Terms
رطب (ratb): Wet or liquid medicine that can flow into the body through a wound.
الفن (al-fan): The anus; via which medicine is inserted into the stomach.
آمنہ (aaminah): The deep interior part of the brain where medicine may reach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, what is the ruling if dry medicine is applied to a wound?
A) It breaks the fast.
B) It does not break the fast.
C) It is makruh.
D) It requires qada.

Key Takeaway
Wet medicine that certainly reaches the stomach or brain invalidates the fast; dry medicine does not.

The Sahibayn's Objection: Certainty of Reaching
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wet medicine and the condition of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding wet medicine and apply the principle of certainty.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn require certainty that the substance reached the interior; without certainty, the fast remains valid.

While the base text rules that wet medicine breaks the fast, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) raise an important objection: there is no certainty that the wet medicine actually reaches the interior. This lesson examines their reasoning and the practical implications of this intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position of the matn
Wet medicine that reaches the interior breaks the fast. This is the apparent ruling from the matn.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's position
They hold that even wet medicine does not break the fast because reaching the interior is not certain.
3
Understand the basis of their disagreement
The Sahibayn emphasize the lack of certainty (yaqin). In Hanafi usul, certainty is required to nullify an act of worship.
This reflects the principle that worship is based on certainty.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the fast is broken and qada is required. Under Sahibayn, the fast remains valid.
This shows the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدواء رطب فوصل الى جوفه او دماغه افطر
اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کوئی چیز بدن میں داخل کرنے سے دوسرے دن روزہ قضا ر کھے۔ البتہ اس دن بھی روزہ پورا کرے چھوڑے نہیں۔ ات الفن : پاخانہ کے راستے سے دوا پیٹ میں ڈالنا۔ آمنہ : دماغ کا گہراز ثم جو دماغ کے اندر تک پہنچ رہا ہو۔ رطب : تر۔ تر دوا کی قید اس لئے لگائی کہ تر دو از خم کی رطوبت کے ساتھ مل کر پیٹ یا دماغ تک پہنچ جاتی ہے۔ جب کہ خشک دوا زخم کی رطوبت کو اور مزید خشک کر دیتی ہے اس لئے وہ آنت تک نہیں پہنچ پاتی۔ اس لئے خشک کے لگانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
نا صاحبین کے نزدیک تر دو الگانے سے بھی روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا کیونکہ آنت تک پہنچنا اور دماغ تک پہنچنا کوئی یقینی نہیں ہے۔ اصول دوایا غذاد ماغ یا پیٹ تک پہنچ جائے تو روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔
With wet medicine that reached his interior or his brain, he breaks fast. From the effect it is known that from something entering the body, the next day he should make up the fast. However, that day he should complete the fast, do not leave it. Al-Fan: inserting medicine into the stomach through the anus. Aamina: the deep part of the brain that reaches the inside of the brain. Ratb: wet. The condition of wet medicine is because wet medicine mixes with the moisture of the wound and reaches the stomach or brain. Whereas dry medicine further dries the wound's moisture, so it does not reach the intestine. Therefore, applying dry medicine does not break the fast. According to the Sahibayn, applying wet medicine also does not break the fast because reaching the intestine and reaching the brain is not certain. The principle: if a medicine or food reaches the stomach or brain, the fast breaks.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn along with explanatory commentary. The ruling is: if a person applies wet medicine to a wound and it reaches the interior of the body (stomach or brain), the fast is broken. The commentary clarifies that 'wet medicine' here refers to liquid preparations that can travel through the wound into the body. Dry medicine, conversely, absorbs wound moisture and does not penetrate to the intestine, so it does not break the fast. The commentary also records a dispute: according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), even wet medicine does not break the fast because there is no certainty that it actually reaches the interior. However, the principle stated at the end affirms that if any substance certainly reaches the stomach or brain, the fast is broken.

Key Terms
رطب (ratb): Wet or liquid medicine that can flow into the body through a wound.
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certainty, a key requirement for nullifying an act of worship according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Instilling Drops in the Urethra
Intra-Hanafi dispute on urethral drops and fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on urethral drops and identify the mufta bihi ruling based on anatomical reasoning.

Core Idea
Liquid in the urethra does not reach the stomach, so it does not break the fast.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute about a medical procedure during fasting. The core question is whether instilling drops into the urethra invalidates the fast. The positions of the three founding Imams are presented, along with their anatomical reasoning, and the conclusion is drawn from modern medical evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the fast is not broken when drops are instilled in the urethra.
This is the majority view within the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf holds that the fast is broken because he believes the urethra has a direct passage to the intestine.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf differs from the other two.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf's dalil is anatomical: he claims that water ingested through the urethra exits through the intestine, indicating a direct connection.
This is a qiyas-based argument using the illa of direct passage.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad counter that the bladder acts as a barrier, so no liquid can reach the intestine. Modern medical research confirms this.
The response uses tahqiq al-manat to verify the illa.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether the fast is valid or must be made up. The mufta bihi is that it remains valid.
Medical evidence has been used as a tarjih mechanism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۷۳] (۲۳) وان اقطر في احليله لم يفطر عند ابی حنفة و محمد وقال ابو يوسف يفطر
اگر پیشاب گاہ میں قطرہ ڈالا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کا نظریہ یہ ہے کہ پیشاب گاہ کے سوراخ کا منفذ آنت تک نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ درمیان میں مثانہ حائل ہے اس سے مترشح ہو کر پیشاب آتا ہے۔ اس لئے کوئی دوایا پانی پیشاب گاہ کے سوراخ میں ڈالے تو وہ آنت تک نہیں پہنچے گی۔ اس لئے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ فائدو امام ابو یوسف کا نظریہ یہ ہے کہ پیشاب گاہ کا سوراخ برارہ راست آنت تک پہنچتا ہے۔ اسی لئے آنت میں گیا ہوا پانی پیشاب کے راستہ سے نکلتا ہے۔ اس لئے جو پانی یا دوا پیشاب گاہ کے سوراخ میں ڈالے گا وہ آنت تک پہنچ جائے گی۔ اس لئے روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔ وت اس مسئلہ کا دارو مدار ڈاکٹری تحقیق پر ہے اور ڈاکٹری تحقیق یہ ہے کہ پیشاب گاہ کا سوراخ برارہ راست آنت تک نہیں ہے اس لئے طرفین کے مسلک کے موافق روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
ان اصیل : پیشاب گاہ کا سوراخ ۔
[573] (23) And if he instills drops in his urethra, he does not break fast according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said he breaks fast. If drops are placed in the urethra, the fast is not broken according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, and Imam Abu Yusuf said the fast is broken. Imam Abu Hanifa's view is that the opening of the urethra does not have a passage to the intestine; rather, the bladder is in between, and urine seeps from it. Therefore, if any medicine or water is placed in the urethra opening, it will not reach the intestine. Hence, the fast is not broken. The benefit: Imam Abu Yusuf's view is that the urethra opening reaches directly to the intestine. For that reason, water that goes into the intestine comes out through the urine path. So if water or medicine is placed in the urethra opening, it will reach the intestine. Hence, the fast is broken. And this matter is dependent on medical research, and medical research is that the urethra opening does not directly reach the intestine, so according to both schools, the fast is not broken. An Asil: the opening of the urethra.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning drops placed in the urethra (preputial opening). The matn states the positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the fast is not broken, while Abu Yusuf holds that it is broken. The commentary explains the rationale: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the urethra does not have a direct passage to the intestine; the bladder acts as a barrier, so any liquid cannot reach the digestive tract. Abu Yusuf argues that the urethra leads directly to the intestine, so liquid can travel to the intestine and break the fast. The commentary then notes that modern medical research confirms that the urethra does not connect directly to the intestine, thus the fast is not broken according to both views. The final relied-upon position is that the fast remains valid.

Key Terms
احليل (ihlil): Urethra; the opening through which urine exits.
قطر (qatr): To instill drops; placing liquid medicine drop by drop into the urethra.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, what is the ruling if one instills drops in the urethra?
A) The fast is broken.
B) The fast is not broken.
C) The fast is makruh.
D) The fast is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The fast is not broken by instilling drops in the urethra because the urethra does not directly connect to the digestive system, as confirmed by modern medical knowledge.

Ruling on a Woman Chewing Food for Her Child While Fasting
Ruling with proof from an athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman chewing food for her child while fasting and identify the condition that makes it permissible or disliked.

Core Idea
Chewing food for a child while fasting is disliked only if an alternative exists; if necessary, it is permitted as long as nothing is swallowed.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario concerning a fasting woman's care for her young child. The matn states the ruling as makruh when an alternative is available, but the commentary and proof clarify that necessity changes the ruling. Understanding the precise limits of this ruling prevents overgeneralization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is a woman chewing food for her child. The default ruling is makruh (disliked) if she has an alternative (i.e., another person can do it, or the child can eat soft food).
'Makruh' here refers to tanzihi (slightly disliked).
2
Note the condition that changes the ruling
The ruling changes to permissible when there is extreme necessity. For example, if no one else can chew and the child cannot swallow unchewed food.
The condition is 'extremely necessary' (ashd zaroorah).
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The proof is an effect (athar) from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba: 'There is no harm that a woman chews for her child while she is fasting, as long as it does not enter her throat.'
The phrase 'la ba's' indicates permissibility, not mere absence of prohibition.
4
Understand the juridical reasoning
The fast is not broken by mere chewing unless food is swallowed. The dislike (karahah) when an alternative exists is due to the risk of swallowing, not an inherent prohibition.
This parallels other cases where actions near the mouth are disliked but not invalidating.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine whether the act is makruh or permissible based on the presence or absence of an alternative. Also, ensure that no food enters the throat.
Practical application: a mother traveling alone with her baby may chew; if her husband is present, it is makruh for her to do so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۷۵] (۲۵) ويكره للمرأة ان تمضغ لصبيها الطعام اذا كان لها منه بد
عورت کے لئے مکروہ ہے کہ اپنے بچے کے لئے کھانا چبائے جب کہ اس کے لئے کوئی راستہ موجود ہو۔ اگر بچے کے کھانے کو چبانے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے تو اس کو چبانا مکروہ ہے۔ اور اگر اشد ضرورت پڑ جائے تو چبا سکتی ہے بشرطیکہ پیٹ میں کھانا نہ جائے۔
اثر میں ہے عن ابراهيم قال لا بأس ان تمضغ المرأة لصبيها وهى صائمة مالم تدخل حلقها (الف)(مصنف ابن الي هيبة ، ۵۰ فی الصائمة تمضغ لصبحاج ثانی ص ۳۰۶، نمبر ۹۲۹۳) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ضرورت پڑے تو عورت اپنے بچے کے لئے کھانا چبا سکتی ہے۔ بشرطیکہ اس کے حلق میں کھانا نہ پہنچے۔
مفع : چبانا۔
[575] (25) And it is disliked for a woman to chew food for her child if she has an alternative. It is disliked for a woman to chew food for her child when she has another way. If there is no need to chew the child's food, then chewing it is makruh. And if there is extreme necessity, she may chew provided that food does not enter the stomach. There is an effect from Ibrahim: 'There is no harm that a woman chews for her child while she is fasting, as long as it does not enter her throat.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 50: Fi al-Sa'imah tamdagh li sabiyyaha, vol. 2 p. 306, no. 9293) From this effect it is known that if need arises, a woman may chew food for her child, provided that food does not reach her throat. Mafa': to chew.

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on a fasting woman chewing food for her young child. The matn states it is disliked for her to do so if she has an alternative (i.e., another person can do it, or the child can eat soft food). The commentary adds that if there is extreme necessity, it is permitted as long as no food enters the throat. The proof is an effect from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, indicating no harm as long as nothing reaches the throat. The act of chewing itself does not break the fast, but it is disliked due to the risk of swallowing.

Ruling on Chewing Gum While Fasting
Ruling with proof for chewing gum while fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on chewing gum while fasting and identify the condition that preserves the validity of the fast.

Core Idea
Chewing gum does not break the fast unless something enters the throat — but it is disliked (makruh tanzih).

This passage addresses a common practice: chewing gum while fasting. It clarifies that the act itself does not invalidate the fast provided no particles are swallowed, but it remains disliked due to the risk of accidental ingestion. The Hanafi position relies on a concession (rukhsah) from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, which explicitly permits chewing gum as long as nothing enters the throat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is chewing gum (madgh al-‘ilk). The ruling stated in the matn is that it does not break the fast (la yufṭir) but is disliked (yukrah).
The dislike is due to the possibility of swallowing small particles, even unintentionally.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites an effect (athar) from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, found in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba. This is a mursal hadith, transmitted from the Successor Ibrahim, who permitted chewing gum for the fasting person.
In Hanafi usul, mursal hadith from the Successors is accepted as proof under certain conditions.
3
Analyze the dalil's indication (wajh al-dalalah)
The word 'رخص' (rakhkhaṣa) indicates a concession (rukhsah), showing that the act is permissible, not prohibited. The condition 'مالم يدخله حلقه' (as long as nothing enters his throat) specifies the boundary of permissibility.
The concession implies that the basic ruling would be prohibition, but the concession overrides it under the condition.
4
Understand the legal implications
The fast remains valid if no part of the gum or its particles reaches the stomach. The dislike (karahah tanzihiyyah) is precautionary, to avoid any potential violation.
If any substance is swallowed intentionally or accidentally, the fast breaks and requires qada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۷۶] (۲۶) ومضغ العلك لا يفطر الصائم ويكره

Gum is chewed by women to clean teeth. Therefore, if one chews just to clean teeth and then spits it out, and no particle enters the throat, the fast is not broken because nothing entered the stomach, but doing so is makruh. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 31: Man rakhkhasa fi madgh al-‘ilk li'l-sa'im, vol. 2 p. 297, no. 9179) From this effect it is known that chewing gum does not break the fast provided that nothing enters the throat. Al-‘ilk: gum used for chewing.

This passage addresses chewing gum (ʿilk) while fasting. The matn rules that it does not break the fast but is disliked (makruh). The commentary explains that gum is primarily used for teeth cleaning. If one chews and then discards it without swallowing any substance, the fast remains valid. The dislike is due to the possibility of swallowing small particles. The proof is an effect from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, explicitly permitting chewing gum for the fasting person as long as nothing enters the throat. The term 'ʿilk' is defined as chewing gum.

Sick Person Fearing Worsening Illness: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on sick person breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who fears worsening of illness and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A sick person who genuinely fears that fasting will worsen their illness may break fast and make it up later — ease is the principle.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute on the conditions for breaking the Ramadan fast due to illness. The Hanafi school holds a broad view of the concession, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts it to extreme cases. We will analyze the Quranic proof and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: a sick person who fears worsening of illness may break fast and make it up. The fear is genuine, not necessarily imminent death.
The hukm level is rukhsah (concession).
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts the concession to cases where death or loss of a limb is feared.
This is the contested element.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i view likely relies on the concept of severe necessity; they interpret 'illness' in the verse as severe illness.
The text does not give their proof explicitly, but it is inferred.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school relies on the general wording of Quran 2:185 and the principle of ease. The verse does not specify a severity threshold.
The Hanafi response is based on the generality of the text.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Hanafi, a person with a moderate but worsening condition may break fast; under Shafi'i, only if life or limb is at risk.
This affects many cases of chronic illness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(Verse 185 of Surah al-Baqarah) From this verse it is known that if there is illness or travel, then he should break fast and make it up on other days. According to Imam Shafi'i, only if there is fear of death or loss of a limb is breaking fast permitted. The commentary adds an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i restricts the concession to cases where fear of death or loss of a limb is present, while the Hanafi position is broader—any genuine fear of increase in illness is sufficient. The footnote explains the verse further, emphasizing ease.

Chewing food for a child is disliked but permissible in necessity if nothing is swallowed.

Fasting mothers may need to pre-chew food for an infant. The Hanafi school addresses this with a conditional ruling: it is makruh when avoidable, but allowed with a key safeguard. This lesson examines the ruling, the evidence from an athar, and the practical condition that preserves the fast's validity.

1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is a fasting woman chewing food for her child. The default ruling from the matn is makruh (disliked) when she has an alternative.

2

The commentator explains that if there is extreme necessity (no alternative), the act becomes permissible, provided nothing reaches her throat.

6 min
Sick Person Fearing Increased Illness May Break Fast
Ruling and Quranic proof for sick person breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who fears increased illness in Ramadan and identify the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
A sick person who fears increased illness may break the fast — Quran 2:185 grants ease.

Ramadan fasting is obligatory, but what if you are sick and fasting could worsen your condition? The Hanafi school, based on the Quran, allows breaking the fast in such cases. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its condition, and the clear evidence from Surah al-Baqarah, along with a comparison with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting for a sick person in Ramadan. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) and recommended to break the fast if one fears increased illness. After recovery, the missed fasts must be made up (qada).
The imperative 'let him break' (افطر) indicates permission and desirability, not obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is Quran 2:185, which explicitly grants a concession for the sick and traveler to fast other days. The verse says 'And whoever is sick or on a journey, then an equal number of other days.'
The Quran is the highest authority (qati al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase is 'then an equal number of other days' (فعدة من أيام أخر). This clearly permits breaking the fast and postponing it, and the concluding phrase 'Allah intends ease for you' supports the Hanafi principle of removing hardship.
Hanafi usul holds that the general command (falyasum) is qualified by the exception for the sick.
4
Note the condition
The condition is 'fears that if he fasts his illness will increase' (فخاف ان صام ازداد مرضة). This fear must be based on prior experience, a doctor's advice, or clear signs. A mere possibility is not sufficient.
Hanafis consider any significant increase in illness sufficient, unlike Shafi'is who require fear of death or loss of a limb.
5
Contrast with other schools
The Shafi'i school requires a higher level of fear (e.g., death or amputation) before breaking is allowed. The Hanafi school takes a broader view, emphasizing ease and preventing harm.
This difference stems from how each school interprets 'fear' (خوف) in the Quranic context.

(٢٥٧) ومن كان مريضا في رمضان فخاف ان صام ازداد مرضة افطر وقضى. جو رمضان میں بیمار ہو، پس خوف کرتا ہو کہ اگر وہ روزہ رکھے گا تو اس کا مرض بڑھ جائے گا تو افطار کرے اور قضا کرے، ان بیمار کو روزہ رکھنے سے بیماری بڑھنے کا خطرہ ہو تو روزہ توڑ سکتا ہے اور بعد میں قضا کرے۔ یہ آیت ہے فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه ومن كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر يريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر. آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ مرض ہو یا سفر ہو تو روزہ توڑے گا اور دوسرے دنوں میں اس کی قضا کرے۔
(257) Whoever is sick in Ramadan and fears that if he fasts his illness will increase, let him break the fast and make it up. And whoever is sick or on a journey, then an equal number of other days. Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship.' From the verse it is understood that if there is illness or travel, he should break the fast and make it up on other days.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a sick person in Ramadan who fears that fasting will increase his illness. The matn states that such a person should break the fast and make it up later. The commentator expands by explaining the condition: there must be a genuine fear based on experience or a reliable doctor's advice. The ruling is based on Quran 2:185, which explicitly grants a concession for the sick and traveler to fast other days. The Hanafi school holds that breaking the fast in such a case is better (afdal) than fasting, as Allah desires ease. The passage also notes that Imam al-Shafi'i requires fear of death or loss of a limb before allowing breaking, while Hanafis are more lenient, considering any significant increase in illness sufficient.

قضاء (qada'): making up missed fasts after Ramadan

Traveler Without Hardship: Fasting Preferred, Breaking Permissible
Ruling on traveler not harmed by fasting: fasting is better, breaking allowed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a traveler who is not harmed by fasting, cite the prophetic evidence, and explain why fasting is considered better (afdal).

Core Idea
A traveler not harmed by fasting may choose: fasting is better, breaking is allowed.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a traveler who does not experience hardship from fasting. Many assume that travel automatically makes breaking the fast mandatory or superior. The Hanafi school, however, distinguishes based on the presence or absence of actual hardship. We will examine the ruling, its evidence from the Prophet's practice, and the underlying reasoning that classifies travel as a general cause of concession even without immediate hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting for a traveler who is not harmed. The ruling: fasting is afdal (better), but breaking and making up is permissible.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas describing the Prophet's journey from Madinah to Makkah where he both fasted and broke his fast.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's actions demonstrate both options, and his statement 'whoever wishes may fast and whoever wishes may break' explicitly grants choice.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools hold that breaking is always better for the traveler; Hanafis say fasting is better when no hardship because of the virtue of Ramadan.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position relies on the hadith and the principle that travel is a cause of hardship, permitting breaking even without actual hardship, but when no hardship, fasting is superior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢٨) وان كان مسافرا لا يستضر بالصوم فصومه افضل وان افطر و قضی جاز۔ اگر مسافر ہے اور روزہ اس کو نقصان نہیں دیتا ہے تو اس کو روزہ رکھنا افضل ہے۔ اور اگر روزہ توڑ دیا اور قضا کیا تو بھی جائز ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن ابن عباس قال حرج رسول الله من المدينة الى مكة فصام حتى بلغ عسفان ثم دعا بماء فرفعه الى يده ليره الناس فافطر حتى قدم مكة وذلك في رمضان فكان ابن عباس يقول قد صام رسول الله وافطر ممن شاء صام ومن شاء افطر. اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مشقت نہ بھی ہو تب بھی مسافر کے لئے گنجائش ہے کہ افطار کرے یا روزہ رکھے۔ سفر میں عموما مشقت ہوتی ہے اس لئے سفر کو مشقت کے درجہ میں رکھ دیا اس لئے مسافر کو روزہ رکھنے میں مشقت نہ بھی ہو تب بھی افطار کر سکتا ہے۔ اور مشقت نہ ہو تو روزہ رکھنا افضل ہے کیونکہ رمضان کی فضیلت بہت بڑی چیز ہے جو بعد میں نہیں ملے گی۔

From this hadith it is understood that even if there is no hardship, the traveler still has the option to break or fast. Since travel generally involves hardship, travel is classified under hardship, so the traveler may break even without actual hardship. But when there is no hardship, fasting is better because the virtue of Ramadan is great and cannot be attained later.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a traveler who is not harmed by fasting. The commentator expands by clarifying that the traveler has a concession to break the fast even without actual hardship because travel is generally a cause of hardship. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas describing the Prophet's practice: he fasted part of a journey and then broke it, showing both options. The Hanafi school views fasting as better when there is no hardship, while breaking is permitted. If hardship exists, breaking is better.

No Makeup Required Upon Death of Sick or Traveler in Their State
Ruling that death in excusing state waives qada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that no qada is required for a sick person or traveler who dies while still in the excusing state, and identify the condition that makes this ruling applicable.

Core Idea
Death while excused waives missed fasts forever — no makeup needed.

This lesson addresses a key dispensation in fasting: when a person misses fasts due to illness or travel and dies before the excuse ends, the obligation to make them up is completely lifted. Understanding this ruling prevents the common misconception that heirs must fast on the deceased's behalf or that fidya is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the persons covered
Note that the ruling applies specifically to two types of individuals: the sick person (al-marid) and the traveler (al-musafir). These are the ones granted a concession to miss fasts during Ramadan.
Only those with a valid excuse; not applicable to someone who deliberately broke the fast without excuse.
2
Confirm the condition at death
The crucial condition is that the person dies 'while in that state' (ala tilka al-halah), meaning their excuse (illness or travel) persists until death. If the excuse ended before death, the ruling changes.
Temporary recovery even for a moment would make qada obligatory.
3
State the ruling
The text explicitly says 'no qada is required of them' (fa la qada 'alayhima). The missed fasts are completely waived; no makeup or compensation is due.
This is the final ruling for such a case; no alternative obligation arises.
4
Understand the legal rationale
The obligation to make up missed fasts arises only when a person has the capacity to do so. Since the excuse continued until death, the capacity never existed, so the obligation never became due. This is based on the principle that obligations are contingent on ability.
This principle is rooted in Quran 2:286: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢٩) وان مات المريض او المسافر وهما على تلك الحالة فلا قضاء عليهما. اگر مریض اور مسافر مر گئے اور دونوں اپنی اپنی حالت پر تھے تو ان دونوں کو قضا لازم نہیں ہے۔ مریض کا مثلا دس روز رمضان کے روزے چھوٹے تھے اور ابھی مرض کی ہی حالت میں تھا، اس کو اس روزے کی قضا کرنے کا موقع نہیں ملا، تو اس پر قضا نہیں۔
(29) And if the sick person or traveler dies while in that state, then no makeup is required of them. If a sick person and a traveler die and both were in their respective states, then no makeup is obligatory upon them. For example, a sick person missed ten days of Ramadan fasts and was still in his state of illness, he did not get the opportunity to make them up, so no qada is upon him.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn: if a sick person or traveler dies while still in the state that excused them from fasting (i.e., before recovering or ending travel), no qada (makeup) is required for the missed fasts. The commentator explains: the missed fasts were not obligatory at the time due to the concession, and since the excuse persisted until death, there is no obligation to make them up. This is based on the principle that obligations are waived if the legal capacity to fulfill them is permanently removed. The ruling is a straightforward statement without additional proof in this passage.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): making up missed obligatory fasts
على تلك الحالة (ala tilka al-halah): in that state, i.e., still sick or still traveling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
If a sick person or traveler dies while still in the excusing state, the obligation to make up missed fasts is completely waived.

Fasting for Traveler and Sick Person: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with hadith proofs on fasting in travel and sickness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a traveler and a sick person regarding Ramadan fasting, and identify the degree of hardship that changes the preference.

Core Idea
Fasting is better for a traveler unless hardship is severe; breaking is always permitted.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on fasting while traveling or sick. The text provides a clear ruling: if fasting worsens illness, one must break; for a traveler not harmed, fasting is preferable but breaking is allowed. Three hadith are cited to support the flexibility and to show that the choice depends on the level of hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Separate the sick person (who is harmed) from the traveler (who is not harmed). Note the distinct rulings: sick must break; traveler may choose.
The sick person's ruling is unconditional if harm occurs.
2
Analyze the Ibn Abbas hadith
The Prophet fasted and broke during travel, showing both are permissible. This establishes the basic license for the traveler.
Even without specific hardship, breaking is allowed.
3
Contrast with the Abu Darda hadith
In extreme heat, the Prophet and Ibn Rawaha fasted, indicating that when hardship is not severe, fasting is better.
Severe hardship triggers a different preference.
4
Apply the Jabir hadith
The Prophet said fasting in travel is not righteousness when hardship is severe, showing that breaking becomes better.
Severe hardship makes fasting makruh tanzihi.
5
Synthesize the principle
The preference depends on degree of hardship: no hardship → fasting better; moderate hardship → still fasting better but breaking allowed; severe hardship → breaking better.
The sick person with harm is treated as severe hardship, making breaking wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
ان صام ازداد مرضة افطر وقضى (۵۷۸ (۲۸) وان كان مسافرا لا يستضر بالصوم فصومه افضل وان افطر و قضی جاز
[۵۷۸] (۲۸) اگر مسافر ہے اور روزہ اس کو نقصان نہیں دیتا ہے تو اس کو روزہ رکھنا افضل ہے۔ اور اگر روزہ توڑ دیا اور قضا کیا تو بھی جائز ہے
(۱) حدیث میں ہے عن ابن عباس قال خرج رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من المدينة الى مكة فصام حتى بلغ عسفان ثم دعا بماء فرفعه الى يده ليريه الناس فافطر حتى قدم مكة وذلك في رمضان فكان ابن عباس يقول قد صام رسول الله وأفطر فمن شاء صام ومن شاء افطر (بخاری شریف، باب من افطر فی السفر ليراه الناس ص ۲۶۱ نمبر ۱۹۴۸، مسلم شریف، باب جواز الصوم والافطار في شهر رمضان للمسافر ص ۳۵۷ نمبر ۱۱۲۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مشقت نہ بھی ہو تب بھی مسافر کے لئے گنجائش ہے کہ افطار کرے یا روزہ رکھے (۲) سفر میں عموما مشقت ہوتی ہے اس لئے سفر کو مشقت کے درجہ میں رکھ دیا اس لئے مسافر کو روزہ رکھنے میں مشقت نہ بھی ہو تب بھی افطار کر سکتا ہے۔ اور مشقت نہ ہو تو روزہ رکھنا افضل ہے کیونکہ رمضان کی فضیلت بہت بڑی چیز ہے جو بعد میں نہیں ملے گی (۲) بعد میں تنہا روزہ قضا کرنے میں پریشانی ہوتی ہے اس لئے بہتر ہے کہ ابھی سب کے ساتھ ادا کر لے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن ابی درداء قال خرجنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في شهر رمضان في حر شديد حتى كان احدنا ليضع يده على رأسه من شدة الحر وما فينا صائم الا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وعبد الله بن رواحة (مسلم شریف، باب جواز الصوم والافطار في شهر رمضان للمسافر ص ۳۵۷ نمبر ۱۱۲۲، ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی اختیار الصیام ص ۳۳۴ نمبر ۲۴۰۹) اس حدیث میں سخت گرمی کے باوجود حضور اور عبد اللہ بن رواحہ نے روزہ رکھا۔ اس لئے کہ رمضان میں مشقت شدیدہ نہ ہو تو روزہ رکھنا افضل ہے۔ مشقت شدیدہ ہو تو افطار کرنا بہتر ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن جابر بن عبد الله عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم راى رجلا يظلل عليه والزحام عليه فقال ليس من البر الصيام في السفر (ابوداؤد شریف، باب اختیار الفطر ص ۳۳۴ نمبر ۲۴۰۷، مسلم شریف، باب جواز الصوم في شهر رمضان للمسافر ص ۳۵۶ نمبر ۱۱۱۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مشقت شدیدہ میں افطار کرنا افضل ہے۔
ان يحضر : ضر سے مشرق ہے نقصان دیتا۔
Book of Fasting: If he fasts and his illness increases, he breaks and makes up. And if he is a traveler who is not harmed by fasting, his fasting is better, and if he breaks and makes up, it is permissible. (578) (28) If a traveler is not harmed by fasting, then fasting is better for him. And if he breaks his fast and makes it up, it is permissible. (1) Hadith: from Ibn Abbas: The Messenger of Allah left Madinah for Makkah and fasted until he reached Usfan, then called for water and raised it to his hand to show the people, then broke his fast until he reached Makkah. That was in Ramadan. Ibn Abbas said: The Messenger of Allah fasted and broke fast, so whoever wishes may fast and whoever wishes may break. From this hadith it is known that even if there is no hardship, the traveler has the option to break or fast. (2) Travel generally involves hardship, so travel is placed in the category of hardship. Therefore, even if there is no hardship in fasting, the traveler may break. And if there is no hardship, then fasting is better because the virtue of Ramadan is great and will not be attained later. Also, making up alone later involves difficulty, so it is better to perform with everyone now. Hadith from Abu Darda: We went out with the Messenger of Allah in Ramadan in intense heat until one would put his hand on his head from heat. None among us was fasting except the Messenger of Allah and Abdullah ibn Rawaha. This hadith shows that despite severe heat, the Prophet and Ibn Rawaha fasted because if there is no severe hardship in Ramadan, fasting is better; if there is severe hardship, breaking is better. Hadith from Jabir: The Prophet saw a man being shaded and crowded, and said: Fasting while traveling is not righteousness. This shows that in severe hardship, breaking is better. 'an yahdur' : from 'durr' meaning harm.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Fasting (Kitab al-Sawm) with supporting hadith. It states two main rulings: (1) If a sick person fasts and his illness increases, he must break his fast and make it up later (qada). (2) For a traveler who does not suffer harm by fasting, fasting is better (afdal), but breaking and making up is also permissible (jaiz). The commentary then explains the rationale and provides three hadith proofs. The first hadith from Ibn Abbas shows that the Prophet himself both fasted and broke fast while traveling, giving a choice. The commentary clarifies that even without specific hardship, the traveler has license to break, but fasting is better due to the superior merit of Ramadan and because making up later involves difficulty. The second hadith from Abu Darda shows that in extreme heat, the Prophet and Ibn Rawaha fasted, indicating that if hardship is not severe, fasting is preferred. The third hadith from Jabir states that fasting in travel is not righteousness when there is severe hardship, indicating that breaking is better in such cases. The passage emphasizes that the ruling depends on the degree of hardship: no hardship → fasting better; severe hardship → breaking better. The note at the end explains the Arabic word 'inhadara' (to suffer harm).

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Fasting in Ramadan, specifically the obligation and its qada
سفر (safar): Travel that is legally considered (masafat al-safar, generally >48 miles)
قضاء (qada): Making up missed fasts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the traveler flexibility: fasting is recommended unless hardship is severe, in which case breaking is better; the sick person must break if harm occurs.

No Qada or Fidyah When Death Occurs During Excuse
Ruling on missed fasts when death occurs during excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick or traveling person who dies before recovering, and apply the principle that qada is only required when there is an opportunity to make up.

Core Idea
No qada or fidyah if death occurs while still in the excuse — obligation only arises when ability exists.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Samiri addresses a critical question: what happens to missed Ramadan fasts if the person dies before the excuse ends? It clarifies that the obligation of qada is contingent upon having the time and ability to perform it. The ruling removes any burden from the deceased and his heirs, provided he never had a chance to make up.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage mentions the sick person (mariḍ) and the traveler (musāfir). These are the two main excuses that permit breaking the fast in Ramadan.
Both must have missed fasts due to their respective excuses.
2
Understand the condition
The ruling applies only when they die 'while in their state' — i.e., still sick or still traveling. If they had recovered or ended travel and died later, the ruling would differ.
The key is whether they had any opportunity to make up the fasts.
3
Extract the ruling
The passage states: no qada (making up) is required for the deceased, and no fidyah (feeding the poor) is required from the heirs.
This is a clear exemption from both the original obligation and any substitute.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The commentary states: 'if there is no time for qada, qada is not necessary.' Obligations in Islam are tied to ability.
Quran 2:286: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.'
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some Shafi'i scholars hold that fidyah is still required even if death occurred during the excuse. Hanafis disagree because the person never had the opportunity to make up.
This difference stems from differing views on whether the obligation of qada lapses with death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۷۹ (۲۹) إذا مات المريض او المسافر وهما على حالهما لم يلزمهما القضاء. إذا مات المريض او المسافر وهما على حالهما لم يلزمهما القضاء. [۵۷۹] (۲۹) اگر مریض اور مسافر مر گئے اور دونوں اپنی اپنی حالت پر تھے تو ان دونوں کو قضا لازم نہیں ہے۔ مریض کا مثلا دس روز رمضان کے روزے چھوٹے تھے اور ابھی مرض کی ہی حالت میں تھا، اس کو اس روزے کی قضا کرنے کا موقع ہی نہیں ملا کہ قضا کر سکے اس لئے اس دس روزے کی قضا کرنا لازم نہیں۔ اور اب موت کے بعد قضا تو نہیں کر سکے گا تو اس کے بدلے ورثہ پر فدیہ دینا بھی لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ یہی حال مسافر کا ہے کہ سفر میں کچھ روزے چھوٹے تھے اور ابھی سفر کی حالت میں تھا کہ انتقال ہو گیا تو چھوٹے ہوئے دنوں کا فدیہ ورثہ پر دین لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ اصول قضا کا وقت نہ ملے تو قضا کرنا لازم نہیں ہے۔
(29) If the sick person or traveler dies while they are in their state, qada is not incumbent upon them. If the sick person or traveler dies while both are in their respective states, qada is not incumbent upon them. For example, a sick person missed ten days of Ramadan fasting and was still in his state of illness, he did not get the opportunity to make up, so qada is not necessary. And after death, he cannot make up, so fidyah is also not incumbent on the heirs. The same is the case for a traveler: he missed some days while traveling and died while still traveling, so fidyah is not necessary on the heirs. Principle: If there is no time for qada, qada is not necessary.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) on the consequence of death while in a state of excuse (illness or travel). It states that if a sick person or a traveler dies while still being sick or traveling respectively, they are not required to make up the missed fasts (qada). The commentary explains the reason: they never had the opportunity to make up the fasts after the excuse ended. Therefore, no qada is required, and no fidyah (compensation feeding) is required from the heirs either. This is based on the principle that if the time for qada never arrived, the obligation of qada does not arise. The passage reinforces that the obligation of qada is contingent upon having the ability and time to perform it after the excuse is removed.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up missed obligatory fasts after Ramadan
فدیہ (fidyah): Feeding a poor person for each missed fast if qada is impossible due to death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a traveler misses ten fasts and dies while still traveling, what is required according to this passage?
A) The heirs must make up the fasts.
B) The heirs must pay fidyah.
C) No qada or fidyah is required.
D) Only qada is required if possible.

Key Takeaway
If a sick or traveling person dies before the excuse ends, no qada or fidyah is required, as the obligation only arises when the ability to make up exists.

Death After Recovery: Fidyah Required for Missed Fasts
Ruling when a sick/traveler recovers then dies without qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a sick or traveler recovers then dies without qada, and explain the proofs from Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
If you can fast but die before making up, your heirs must feed the poor for each missed day.

This lesson examines a key scenario in fasting: a person who misses fasts due to illness or travel, then recovers or ends travel, has an opportunity to make up. If that person dies before fulfilling qada, what is due? The Hanafi school, based on a clear hadith and Quranic principle, requires the heirs to give fidyah (feeding) for each day the deceased had a chance to make up. We'll walk through the ruling, its evidence, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is missing fasts due to illness or travel. The ruling: once the excuse ends (recovery/residence), qada becomes wajib. If death occurs before making up, fidyah is wajib from the heirs.
Fidyah is one meal per missed day (half sa' of wheat or one sa' of dates/barley).
2
Understand the condition
Only the days after recovery or residence are counted for fidyah, not the entire missed period. The text states 'بقدر الصحة والاقامة' (to the extent of health and residence).
Example: 10 days missed, recovered for 5 days then died → fidyah for 5 days.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
Verse 2:185 commands making up fasts on other days after the excuse ends. This implies immediate obligation when able.
The verse does not specify what happens after death; the hadith provides the detail.
4
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith from Ubadah ibn Nusi explicitly distinguishes two cases: continuous sickness until death (no feeding) vs. recovery then death (feeding required). This is the direct textual basis for the Hanafi position.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted for this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۸۰] (۳۰) وان صح المريض او اقام المسافر ثم ماتا لزمهما القضاء بقدر الصحة والاقامة. [۵۸۰] (۳۰) اگر مریض تندرست ہو جائے یا مسافر مقیم ہو جائے پھر دونوں مرجائے تو دونوں کی صحت کی مقدار اور اقامت کی مقدار قضا لازم ہوگی۔ مثلا مرض کی حالت میں دس روز رمضان کے روزے چھوٹے تھے۔ اب وہ پانچ روز صحت یاب ہوا اور روزہ قضا کر سکتا تھا لیکن قضا نہیں کیا اور انتقال ہو گیا تو پانچ روز کی قضا لازم ہے۔ لیکن موت کے بعد قضا نہیں کر سکتا تو ورثہ پر ان پانچ روزوں کا فدیہ دینا لازم ہوگا۔ یہی حال مسافر کا ہے۔ آیت میں ہے ومن كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر (آیت ۱۸۵ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ رمضان میں روزہ نہ رکھ سکے تو جب فرصت ہو تو روزہ رکھے۔ اور اس کو فرصت ہو چکی تھی، تندرستی آچکی تھی اس لئے روزہ رکھنا چاہئے تھا۔ اور نہیں رکھا تو قضا لازم ہوگی۔ (۲) حدیث میں ہے عن عبادة بن نسي قال قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من مرض في رمضان فلم يزل من مريضا حتى مات لم يطعم عنه وان صح فلم يقضه حتى مات اطعم عنه (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب المريض في رمضان وقضاء ج رابع ص ۲۳۷ نمبر ۷۶۳۵) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ پہلے رمضان کی قضا کرنا چاہئے۔ اور نہ کر سکا تو ورثہ اس کا فدیہ دیں گے۔ کیونکہ قضا کا وقت ملا تھا۔
(30) If the sick person recovers or the traveler becomes resident, then they die, qada is incumbent upon them to the extent of the recovery or residence period. If the sick person recovers or the traveler becomes resident, then both die, qada is incumbent upon them to the extent of health and residence. For example, a sick person missed ten days of Ramadan. He then became healthy for five days and could have made up, but did not, and died. Then five days of qada are due. But after death he cannot make up, so fidyah is due from the heirs. The same is for the traveler. The verse: 'And whoever is sick or on a journey, then a number of other days' (al-Baqarah 2:185). This verse indicates that if one cannot fast in Ramadan, then fast when free. And he had opportunity, health came, so he should have fasted. Since he did not, qada is due. (2) Hadith from Ubadah ibn Nusi: The Prophet said: Whoever is sick in Ramadan and remains sick until death, no feeding is due from him; but if he recovers and does not make up until death, then feeding is due from him. This hadith shows that qada of Ramadan should be done, and if unable, heirs feed because time was available.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a sick person who recovers or a traveler who ends his travel, then dies without making up missed fasts. The key principle is that once the excuse is removed (health or residence), the obligation to make up (qada) becomes due. If the person dies after having a reasonable opportunity to make up but before doing so, the missed fasts must be made up by the heirs in the form of fidyah (feeding a poor person for each missed day). The passage provides two proofs: the Quranic verse (2:185) which commands making up on other days after the excuse ends, and a hadith from Ubadah ibn Nusi that explicitly differentiates between dying while sick (no feeding) and recovering then dying (feeding required). The commentary uses the example of a sick person who had ten days missed, recovered for five days, had the chance to make up but didn't, then died; only five days are due as fidyah because only those five days had opportunity for qada. The same logic applies to the traveler.

Key Terms
فدیہ (fidyah): Compensation feeding for missed fasts when qada is impossible due to death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person missed 12 days of Ramadan due to sickness, recovered for 3 days, then died. How many days of fidyah are due?
A) 12 days
B) 3 days
C) 0 days
D) 15 days

Key Takeaway
Fidyah is due from heirs only for the days after the excuse ended, not for the entire missed period.

Spacing of Qada: Consecutive or Separate
Ruling on optionality of consecutive or separate qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether qada of Ramadan fasts must be consecutive and identify the evidence for the permissibility of both methods.

Core Idea
Qada of Ramadan fasts may be performed consecutively or separately — both are equally permissible.

This lesson presents a straightforward ruling from the Hanafi matn: when making up missed Ramadan fasts, you have complete flexibility. The text states that you may fast them consecutively or separately, as you wish. This removes the common misconception that qada must mimic the original uninterrupted month.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act in question? Qada (making up missed Ramadan fasts). What is the hukm for the method? Mubah (permissible) – both consecutive and separate are allowed without preference.
The ruling is about the method, not the obligation of qada itself.
2
Understand the two options
Consecutive (tabi'): fasting day after day without break. Separate (farraqa): fasting with intervals. The example of ten missed days illustrates: you may complete them in ten continuous days or spread them out (e.g., two days now, four later, etc.).
Any combination that totals the missed days is valid.
3
Examine the evidence
The hadith from Ibn Umar (cited via Darqutni) explicitly gives the choice: 'If he wishes, let him separate; if he wishes, let him follow consecutively.' This is the direct proof.
The hadith is a clear textual evidence (nass) for permissibility.
4
Note the legal implication
No preference or obligation exists between the two methods. The individual may choose based on convenience.
This flexibility is a mercy (rukhsa) from Allah.
5
Cross-reference with other schools
While Hanafis and others (Shafi'is, Malikis) allow separation, some early scholars (e.g., some Zahiris) required consecutiveness. The Hanafi position relies on the explicit hadith.
This is not a dispute within the Hanafi school; all three imams agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۸۱] (۳۱) وقضاء رمضان ان شاء فرقه وان شاء تابع. [۵۸۱] (۳۱) قضاء رمضان چاہے تو الگ الگ کر کے رکھے چاہے تو مسلسل رکھے۔ مثلا دس روز رمضان کے روزے قضا ہوئے تھے تو یہ بھی جائز ہے کہ مسلسل دس روز روزے رکھ کر پورے کرے اور یہ بھی جائز ہے کہ دو روز رکھے پھر کچھ دنوں کے بعد چار روزے رکھے اور تفریق کر کے دس روز پورے کرے۔ عن ابن عمر ان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال في قضاء رمضان ان شاء فرق وان شاء تابع (دارقطنی، باب القبلة للصائم ص ...)
(31) And the qada of Ramadan: if he wishes, he may make them separate, and if he wishes, consecutive. Qada of Ramadan: if he wishes, he may make them separate, and if he wishes, consecutive. For example, ten days of Ramadan were missed; it is permissible to make them up consecutively in ten continuous days, and also permissible to make two days, then after a few days four days, and so on by separating until ten days are completed. From Ibn Umar: The Prophet said regarding qada of Ramadan: If he wishes, let him separate, and if he wishes, let him follow consecutively. (Darqutni)
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling (hukm) on the method of making up missed Ramadan fasts (qada). The text explicitly states that qada may be performed either consecutively (mutabiq) or separately (mutafarriq). The commentary gives an example: ten missed days can be made up in ten continuous days or spread out over time. A hadith from Ibn Umar is cited as proof, where the Prophet explicitly gave the choice. This ruling provides flexibility, removing any misconception that qada must be continuous. It is a matn-type statement meant for memorization: 'وقضاء رمضان ان شاء فرقه وان شاء تابع'.

Key Terms
تابع (tabi'): Consecutive, one after another without break
فرق (farraqa): To separate, to make up non-consecutively

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith quoted, what does the Prophet say about qada of Ramadan?
A) It must be consecutive
B) It must be separate
C) You have the choice to make them consecutive or separate
D) It must be separate unless you specify otherwise

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that qada of Ramadan fasts may be performed consecutively or separately, based on the explicit choice given by the Prophet.

Qada and Fidyah Upon Death After Recovery or Residence
Ruling on qada after recovery and death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for a sick or traveling person who recovers/resides and then dies, and apply the condition that triggers fidyah upon the heirs.

Core Idea
Opportunity to fast after recovery makes qada wajib; if death intervenes, fidyah replaces it for those days.

This passage clarifies the obligation of making up missed Ramadan fasts when a person had a window of ability but did not act, then died. It distinguishes between those who never had the chance (no fidyah) and those who did (fidyah on heirs). The ruling is supported by Quranic permission to postpone and a hadith specifying the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes two cases: a sick person who recovers and a traveler who becomes resident, then both die. Note that they had a period of health/residence after the Ramadan during which they could have made up missed fasts.
The key is the existence of an opportunity to fast after the excuse ends.
2
State the ruling
Qada (making up) is wajib upon them for the number of days they could have fasted. After death, the heirs must give fidyah (feeding a poor person) for those days.
The fidyah is not for all missed days, only those with opportunity.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 2:185 (permission to postpone) and a hadith from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq that distinguishes between continuous sickness (no fidyah) and recovery without qada (fidyah).
The hadith directly supports the condition: if he recovers and does not make up, fidyah is due.
4
Trace wajh al-dalalah
From the Quran, the command to fast on other days implies that when the excuse ends, qada becomes an immediate obligation. The hadith makes the obligation of fidyah conditional on having had the ability to make up.
Thus, the Hanafi position uses these texts to derive conditional liability.
5
Note cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that if a person dies before making up missed fasts, fidyah is due regardless of whether they had an opportunity. The Hanafi school limits fidyah to days after the opportunity arose.
The practical difference: under Hanafi, heirs may pay less fidyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۵۸۰] (۳۰) وان صح المريض او اقام المسافر ثم ماتا لزمهما القضاء بقدر الصحة والاقامة [۵۸۰] (۳۰) اگر مریض تندرست ہو جائے یا مسافر مقیم ہو جائے پھر دونوں مرجائے تو دونوں کی صحت کی مقدار اور اقامت کی مقدار قضا لازم ہوگی۔ شر مثلا مرض کی حالت میں دس روز رمضان کے روزے چھوٹے تھے۔ اب وہ پانچ روز صحت یاب ہوا اور روزہ قضا کر سکتا تھا لیکن قضا نہیں کیا اور انتقال ہو گیا تو پانچ روز کی قضا لازم ہے۔ لیکن موت کے بعد قضا نہیں کر سکتا تو ورثہ پر ان پانچ روزوں کا فدیہ دینا لازم ہوگا۔ یہی حال مسافر کا ہے۔ آیت میں ہے ومن كان مريضا او على سفر فعدة من ايام اخر (الف) ( آیت ۱۸۵سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ رمضان میں روزہ نہ رکھ سکے تو جب فرصت ہو تو روزہ رکھے۔ اور اس کو فرصت ہو چکی تھی ،تندرستی آچکی تھی اس لئے روزہ رکھنا چاہئے تھا۔ اور نہیں رکھا تو قضا لازم ہوگی (۲) حدیث میں ہے عن عبادة بن نسى قال قال النبي الا الله من مرض في رمضان فلم يزل الا من مريضا حتى مات لم يطعم عنه وان صح فلم يقضه حتى مات اطعم منه (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب المريض في رمضان وقضاء ج رابع ص ۲۳۷ نمبر ۷۶۳۵ ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ پہلے رمضان کی قضا کرنا چاہئے۔ اور نہ کر سکا تو درشتہ اس کا فدیہ دیں گے ۔ کیونکہ قضا کا وقت ملا تھا۔
[580] (30) And if the sick person recovers or the traveler becomes resident, then both die, qada is incumbent upon them to the extent of health and residence. [580] (30) For example, a sick person missed ten days of Ramadan fasts. He became healthy for five days and could have made up the fasts but did not, and then died. Qada is necessary for five days. But after death he cannot make up, so the heirs must give fidyah for those five days. The same applies to the traveler. In the verse: 'And whoever is sick or on a journey, then (complete) a number from other days' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:185). From this verse it is understood that if he cannot fast in Ramadan, he should fast when he gets a chance. He had the chance; health had come, so he should have fasted. Since he did not, qada is necessary. (2) Hadith: From Ubadah ibn Nasi, he said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever falls sick in Ramadan and remains sick until he dies, nothing is fed on his behalf. But if he recovers and does not make up until he dies, then food is fed on his behalf.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-marid fi Ramadan wa qada'ihi, vol. 4, p. 237, no. 7635). This hadith indicates that one must make up the previous Ramadan, and if he cannot, then his heirs will give fidyah, because he had time to make up.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the obligation of qada (make-up fasts) when a sick person recovers or a traveler becomes resident and then dies. The ruling states that they are liable for qada proportional to the period of health or residence after which they could have fasted but did not. Upon death, the heirs must give fidyah for those days. The commentary provides an example: if someone missed ten days of Ramadan due to sickness, then recovered for five days but did not make them up before dying, the heirs must give fidyah for those five days. The proof is from the Quran (2:185) which commands fasting on other days after recovery, and a hadith that distinguishes between one who remains sick until death (no fidyah) and one who recovers but neglects qada until death (fidyah required).

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up missed obligatory fasts after Ramadan
فدية (fidyah): Feeding a poor person for each missed fast, required when qada is permanently impossible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No fidyah, because he was sick.
B) Heirs must give fidyah for the 5 days he could have fasted.
C) Heirs must fast for him.
D) Nothing; the qada is waived.

Key Takeaway
Opportunity to fast after the excuse ends triggers the obligation of qada and, upon death without fulfilling it, fidyah on the heirs.

The Flexibility of Qada: Separate or Consecutive
Ruling on performing make-up fasts separately or continuously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing qada fasts separately or consecutively, cite the hadith proof, and explain the practical implications.

Core Idea
Both separate and consecutive qada fasts are valid — the Prophet explicitly permitted both.

This lesson addresses a common question about make-up fasts: must they be done continuously or can they be spread out? The Hanafi school, relying on a clear hadith, affirms that both methods are permissible. We will examine the proof and its implications for daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qada (make-up fasting) for missed Ramadan days. The Hanafi ruling is that performing this obligation either separately or consecutively is permissible (mubah). The underlying obligation is wajib, but the method is flexible.
This permissibility is part of the school's emphasis on ease (taysir).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith from Ibn Umar (ra) reported by Darqutni, where the Prophet (saw) said: 'If he wishes he may separate them, and if he wishes he may join them.'
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator) but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit permission phrase 'in sha'a farraqa wa in sha'a tabi'a' indicates that both options are legally valid. The command for qada is satisfied regardless of the pattern.
No additional condition or restriction is attached.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and thamarah
While some scholars recommend continuity (tatabi') for avoiding delay, the Hanafi position is that both are equally valid. The practical consequence is that one may fast whenever convenient, as long as qada is completed before the next Ramadan.
Delaying without excuse may be makruh, but the method itself remains permissible.

[581] (31) Qada of Ramadan: if he wishes he may perform the fasts separately, and if he wishes he may perform them consecutively. For example, if ten days of Ramadan were missed, it is permissible to fast ten consecutive days to complete them, or to fast two days, then after some days fast four days, and by separation complete the ten days. From Ibn Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said regarding qada of Ramadan: 'If he wishes he may separate them, and if he wishes he may join them.' (Darqutni, Bab al-qibla lil-sa'im, footnote). This hadith indicates that qada can be done with intervals or consecutively.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that make-up fasts for Ramadan can be performed either separately (with gaps) or continuously. The commentary explains with an example: if ten fasts are due, one may fast ten consecutive days or spread them out, e.g., two days, then later four days, etc. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Umar that explicitly permits both options. The passage also notes that performing them consecutively is recommended to clear the obligation quickly.

Key Terms
تابع (tabi'a): Consecutive, continuous (fasting without break)
فرق (farraqa): Separate, with intervals

Delaying Qada Until Next Ramadan
Ruling with proof from a Companion athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delaying qada fasts until the next Ramadan, explain the evidence from the athar of Abu Huraira, and distinguish the recommended feeding from obligatory fidyah.

Core Idea
Fasting the current Ramadan takes priority over qada, and delay alone incurs no fidyah.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a person who has missed fasts from a previous Ramadan but has not made them up before the next Ramadan arrives. The ruling clarifies the correct order—fast the current Ramadan on time, then make up the old ones—and rejects any obligatory compensation for the delay alone. The proof is traced to a Companion report (athar) that the Hanafi school employs as evidence. Understanding this ruling helps avoid confusion about priorities and penalties in fasting obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is delaying the making up (qada) of missed Ramadan fasts until the next Ramadan arrives. The ruling specifies the order: fast the current Ramadan first, then make up the previous ones. No fidyah is required for the delay.
The ruling differentiates between the obligation of the current Ramadan and the ongoing obligation of qada.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is an athar (report) from Abu Huraira in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq. The text is: 'Let him fast the one that has come, then make up the latter and feed a poor person for each day.' This athar is used as a proof in the Hanafi school.
Athar is considered a proof (hujja) in Hanafi usul, though it is zanni in transmission.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof conveys the ruling)
The command to fast the current Ramadan first indicates priority. The mention of feeding is interpreted by the Hanafi school as recommended (istihbab), not obligatory, because the obligation of qada remains intact and is not penalized by delay alone.
The wording 'let him feed' (yut'im) could be imperative, but Hanafi usul distinguishes between commands for obligation and recommendation based on context and other proofs.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
Consider a person who missed 5 days of Ramadan 1445 Hijri and did not make them up before Ramadan 1446. According to this ruling, he must fast the entire month of Ramadan 1446 as usual, and after Eid, he must make up the 5 missed days from 1445. He does not owe any fidyah for the delay.
If he were to die before making up, then the qada obligation would be handled by his heirs or fidyah from the estate, but that is a separate ruling.
5
Distinguish the recommended feeding from obligatory fidyah
The athar mentions feeding a poor person for each day, but the Hanafi school classifies this as mustahabb (recommended), not wajib. Fidyah (compensatory feeding) is only due when fasting becomes permanently impossible, such as in chronic illness or death. Mere delay does not trigger fidyah.
This distinction is crucial: students must not conflate a supererogatory act with an obligatory one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[582] (32) And if he delays it until the next Ramadan enters, he fasts the second Ramadan and makes up the first after it, and there is no fidyah upon him. [582] (32) Regarding the delay of Ramadan's qada until the second Ramadan comes: he will fast the second Ramadan's fast, and make up the first Ramadan's qada afterwards, and there is no fidyah on him. For example, a person had some qada fasts from Ramadan but kept delaying until the next year's Ramadan came. He should perform the current Ramadan's fasts now, and after this Ramadan he will make up the previous year's qada. (1) The previous year's fasts are already qada; if he delays the current year, they too will become qada. So he should perform the current year's fasts on time and make up the previous year's qada later. (2) There is an athar from Abu Huraira: he said: 'A person fell sick in Ramadan, then recovered, and did not make up until he encountered another Ramadan. Let him fast the one that has come, then make up the latter [i.e., the previous one] and feed a poor person for each day.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-marid fi Ramadan wa qada'ihi, vol. 4, p. 234, no. 7621). From this athar it is understood that he will fast the second Ramadan first and make up the first Ramadan's qada later. And fidyah is not necessary because whenever he makes up, it is still qada; therefore no fidyah is due. The mention in the athar of feeding a poor person each day is by way of istihbab (recommendation), not as compulsory fidyah.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This ruling addresses a person who delays making up missed Ramadan fasts until the next Ramadan arrives. The correct procedure is to first fast the current Ramadan (the one that has arrived) on time, and then make up the previous Ramadan's missed fasts after the current Ramadan ends. No fidyah is incurred for the delay alone. The proof is an athar from Abu Huraira which indicates this order and also mentions feeding a poor person each day, but the commentator clarifies that this feeding is recommended (istihbab), not obligatory. The reason no fidyah is required is that the qada obligation remains and can still be fulfilled; it is not considered a permanent inability.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): A saying or report from a Companion (here Abu Huraira) used as evidence
فدية (fidyah): Compensatory feeding required when qada is permanently impossible; not required here

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fast the 5 days first, then fast Ramadan 2025.
B) Fast Ramadan 2025 first, then make up the 5 days after.
C) Pay fidyah for the 5 days and do not fast.
D) Fast 5 days during Ramadan 2025 along with it.

Key Takeaway
When qada is delayed until the next Ramadan, fast the current Ramadan first, then make up the previous, and no fidyah is required; the feeding mentioned in the athar is recommended, not obligatory.

Pregnant and Nursing Women: No Fidyah, Only Qada
Ruling with proof for pregnant/nursing women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for pregnant and nursing women who fear for their child, and explain why they must make up fasts but owe no fidyah.

Core Idea
Pregnant and nursing women break fast, make up later, and owe no fidyah.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in Ramadan: a pregnant or nursing woman fears harm to her child if she fasts. The Hanafi school gives a clear ruling that balances the child's safety with the obligation of Ramadan. The ruling distinguishes these women from the elderly who cannot fast at all, and classifies them like the sick or traveler who have a temporary excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that pregnant and nursing women, when they fear for their child, are to break the fast and make up later, with no fidyah. Identify the act (breaking fast) and the consequences (qada wajib, no fidyah).
The ruling applies only when there is genuine fear of harm to the child.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The commentary explains that these women are like the sick or traveler: they have a temporary excuse but can make up later. Therefore, they only owe qada, not fidyah. Contrast with the elderly (shaykh fani) who have no hope of recovery and thus pay fidyah.
The key distinction is the ability to fast later.
3
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith from Anas ibn Malik (though cut off). In Hanafi fiqh, such ahad hadith are acceptable as proof for rulings provided they meet the conditions of authenticity.
The hadith is not fully quoted, but it is used as a proof for the permission and qada.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (difference of opinion)
The hanafi_position_box indicates that some schools may require fidyah in addition to qada for nursing women. The Hanafi school holds only qada. This difference stems from the categorization: are they like the sick (only qada) or like the elderly (fidyah)?
The practical result: Hanafi mufti gives no fidyah ruling; other schools may give fidyah.
5
Apply the ruling to a case
Now apply: A pregnant woman in the first trimester is advised by her doctor that fasting may cause dehydration affecting the fetus. She breaks her fast. According to Hanafi, she must make up the missed days later, and she owes no fidyah.
Ensure fear is based on reasonable evidence, not mere assumption.

Source Text

Delaying Qada of Ramadan Until the Next Ramadan
Ruling on delaying qada fasts until next Ramadan with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delaying qada fasts until the next Ramadan and explain why no fidyah is required.

Core Idea
Delaying qada until next Ramadan does not incur fidyah; the current Ramadan takes priority over past qada.

When a person misses Ramadan fasts and delays making them up until the next Ramadan arrives, a clear sequence of obligations emerges. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling: you must fast the current Ramadan on time, then make up the missed fasts afterwards. No expiation (fidyah) is due for the delay alone. We will examine the evidence—a hadith and an athar—and see how the Hanafi school interprets them to uphold this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the situation: a person has missed fasts from a previous Ramadan and has not made them up before the next Ramadan begins.
This excludes those permanently unable to fast.
2
Determine the Sequence of Fasting
The person must first fast the current Ramadan (the one that has just arrived) on time. Then, after Eid, they must make up the missed fasts from the previous Ramadan.
This order ensures the current obligation is fulfilled first.
3
Rule on Fidyah
No fidyah is required for the delay. The missed fasts remain a debt (dayn) to be made up; mere delay does not trigger compensation.
Fidyah only applies to those permanently unable to fast (e.g., elderly or chronically ill).
4
Understand the Proof and Its Interpretation
The hadith from Dar Qutni recommends consecutive fasting but does not obligate fidyah. The athar from Abd al-Razzaq mentions feeding a poor person, but the Hanafi school interprets this as a recommendation (istihbab), not an obligation.
The Hanafi usul principle: al-amr yufidu al-nadb (command indicates recommendation) when there is opposing evidence.
